Conditions for Valid Divorce: Sanity, Adulthood, and Coercion
Rulings on who can validly pronounce divorce and effect of coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid divorce in the Hanafi school and explain why a coerced divorce is valid despite the lack of consent.

Core Idea
A coerced divorce is valid because the hadith on seriousness overrides the excuse of coercion.

Divorce in Islam is a serious matter, but what happens when a husband is forced to say the words? This ruling from the Hanafi school clarifies the conditions of the person pronouncing divorce and explains why coercion does not invalidate the pronouncement, contrary to other schools. We'll examine the proofs and the reasoning behind this position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditions for a valid divorce regarding the husband's state
From the passage, isolate the prerequisites: sanity, adulthood, and being awake. Note that minors and the insane are excluded.
The text also equates the imbecile and unconscious to the insane.
2
Locate the Hanafi proof for the validity of coerced divorce
Two proofs are cited: the general hadith 'every divorce is permissible' and the specific hadith that three things are serious whether in jest or earnest.
The second hadith is given in full: ثلاث جدهن جد وهزلهن جد: النكاح والطلاق والرجعة.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah of the hadith
The hadith shows that divorce is effective irrespective of intent (jest or serious). By extension, coercion does not negate effectiveness since the utterance is still made.
Hanafi usul: 'al-amr yufidu al-wujub' but here it's about absence of intent mattering.
4
Compare the opposing view (Shafi'i et al.) and their proof
The other school uses the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان وما استكرهوا عليه' to argue that coerced actions are pardoned.
The Hanafi response: that hadith refers to rulings of the afterlife, not worldly legal effects.
5
Understand the Hanafi response and why this ruling is mufta bihi
The Hanafi position is considered mufta bihi because of the strength of the proof texts and the listing of twenty actions valid under coercion.
The text cites Fath and Ayni as supporting authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عاقل بالغ: [فلا يقع طلاق الصبي ولو كان البلوغ بالسن والشرط أن يكون الزوج مستيقظاً. (ط،ع)] لقوله عليه "كل طلاق جائز إلا طلاق الصبي والمجنون وحكم المعتوه والمغمى عليه كالمجنون، وكذا لا يقع طلاق النائم، والمعتوه كان قليل الفهم مختلط الكلام فاسد التدبير إلا أنه لا يضرب ولا يشتم. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٤/١] ولو مكرهاً متصلة، أي وإن كان الزوج مكرها على إنشاء الطلاق، أما لو أكره على الإقرار بالطلاق فأقر لا ينفذ إقراره. (ط)] أي بالنطق فلو أكره بالحبس والضرب على أن يكتب طلاق امرأته فكتب لا تطلق امرأته؛ لأن الكتابة أقيمت مقام العبارة باعتبار الحاجة ولا حاجة هنا، وقال الشافعي طلاق المكره لا يقع؛ لقوله عليا "رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان وما استكرهوا عليه" وبه قال مالك وأحمد، ولنا: عموم ما روينا من أن كل طلاق جائز وأيضاً قوله عليا ثلاث جدهن جد وهزلهن جد: النكاح والطلاق والرجعة"، رواه الطحاوي وغيره، وقال الترمذي: حديث حسن، وخرجه الحاكم في "المستدرك". وقال : صحيح الإسناد، والمراد بما رواه أحكام الآخرة؛ لأن عينه ليس بمراد لوجوده حقيقة، وحكمه دنيوي وأخروي، فلا يتناولهما لفظ واحد؛ لأنهما كالمشترك، وحكم الآخرة مراد بالإجماع فانتفى الآخر أن يكون مراداً ثم جملة ما يصح من الأحكام مع الإكراه عشرون الطلاق والنكاح والرجعة والحلف بطلاق أو إعتاق والظهار والإيلاء والعتق وإيجاب الصدقة والعفو عن دم العمد وقبول المرأة الطلاق على مال والإسلام وقبول الصلح عن العمد على مال والتدبير والاستيلاد والرضاع واليمين والنذر والفيء والإكراه على قبول الوديعة. (فتح، عيني)
Sane, adult: [The divorce of a boy does not occur even if he reaches puberty by age, and the condition is that the husband is awake. (Tatawkhani, Ayni)] Due to his statement عليه السلام 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a boy and an insane person.' The ruling of an imbecile (maʿtūh) and one who is unconscious is like that of an insane person. Likewise, the divorce of a sleeping person does not occur. The imbecile is one who has little understanding, confused speech, and poor management, but he is not beaten or insulted. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/234] And even if he is coerced continuously, meaning even if the husband is coerced to initiate divorce, but if he is coerced to acknowledge divorce and he acknowledges, his acknowledgment is not effective. (Tatawkhani)] Meaning verbally. So if he is coerced by imprisonment and beating to write a divorce for his wife and he writes, his wife is not divorced because writing is established in place of speech due to necessity, but there is no necessity here. Al-Shafi'i said: The divorce of a coerced person does not occur due to his statement عليه السلام 'My community is pardoned for mistake, forgetfulness, and what they are coerced into.' Malik and Ahmad also said this. Our proof is the general meaning of what we narrated: that every divorce is permissible, and also his statement عليه السلام: 'Three things are serious whether serious or joking: marriage, divorce, and revoking divorce (rajʿah).' Reported by al-Tahawi and others. Al-Tirmidhi said: It is a hasan hadith. Al-Hakim narrated it in al-Mustadrak and said it is sahih. The meaning of what he narrated is the rulings of the hereafter; because the literal wording is not intended due to its existence in reality, and its ruling is worldly and otherworldly, so a single word does not include both because they are like homonyms. The otherworldly ruling is intended by consensus, so the other is negated from being intended. Then the total of what is valid from rulings under coercion is twenty: divorce, marriage, revocation, oath by divorce or manumission, zihar, ila, manumission, obligation of sadaqah, pardon for intentional blood, woman's acceptance of divorce for compensation, Islam, acceptance of sulh for intentional blood for compensation, tadbir, istilad, radaʿ, oath, vow, fay', and coercion to accept a deposit. (Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 400

Commentary
This passage states the conditions for a valid divorce regarding the person of the husband: he must be sane (aql) and adult (bulugh), and even coercion does not invalidate his pronouncement. The text first cites the hadith 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a boy and an insane person' to exclude minors and the insane. It then discusses coercion: Abu Hanifa holds that a coerced divorce is valid, contrary to al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad. The Hanafi proof is the hadith that divorce is serious even in jest, and the general principle that a coerced utterance is effective in matters like divorce. The text then lists twenty actions that remain valid under coercion, emphasizing that divorce is one of them. The commentary also clarifies that writing under coercion does not effect divorce because writing is only a substitute when there is necessity, which is absent here.

Key Terms
مكرهاً (mukrahan): One who is coerced into pronouncing divorce through threat of harm, but still valid according to Hanafi.
صبي (sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty; his divorce is invalid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school presented in this passage, what is the ruling on a divorce pronounced under coercion?
A) It does not occur because intent is lacking.
B) It occurs and is valid.
C) It occurs only if the coercion involved physical harm.
D) It does not occur if the coercion was verbal threat.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, a divorce pronounced under coercion is valid because the seriousness of the act overrides the lack of consent, as established by hadith.

Divorce of the Drunkard (Sakran) in Hanafi Law
Ruling with proof and internal dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the validity of a drunk's divorce, explain the deterrent basis, and identify the internal disagreements.

Core Idea
A drunk's divorce is valid to deter sin, despite loss of intellect.

This lesson examines a controversial case: the divorce uttered by a drunkard. While other schools invalidate it due to lack of sanity, the Hanafi school upholds its validity based on a policy of deterrence and the drunkard's culpability in his condition. We will explore the ruling, its proofs, and variations within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
The passage begins with the general pronouncement that a drunkard's divorce occurs. Note that this is the Hanafi position presented first.
Contrast with the boy, insane, and sleeper whose divorces are invalid.
2
Recognize the inter-madhab dispute
The text immediately cites al-Shafi'i and Ahmad, who hold the divorce does not occur because it depends on sound intellect which is absent. This is the opposing view.
The passage also notes that early Hanafis like al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi agreed with that view.
3
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on intoxicant type
For drinks made from grains or honey, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf rule no divorce, while Muhammad rules it occurs based on whether the drink is haram.
This shows that the ruling can be conditional on the cause of intoxication.
4
Understand the rationale of deterrence
The relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) is that the divorce occurs as a deterrent (zajran). The drunkard is not excused because he brought the intoxication upon himself through sin.
The passage mentions al-Kirmani and the text al-Uyun as sources for this fatwa.
5
Apply the rule to agents and special cases
If a drunk acts as an agent for divorce, the divorce may not occur depending on whether compensation was involved. This refines the general rule.
The passage ends this section with a ruling on agency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسكران، وأخرس بإشارته حرّاً أو عبداً ، لا طلاق الصبي والمجنون والنائم، والسيد أي لا يقع على امرأة عبده، ولو مملوكة للسيد وسكران [عطف على قوله: "مكرها أي ولو كان الزوج سكران. (ع)] وعند الشافعي في قول، وأحمد في رواية: لا يقع طلاقه، وقد نقل ذلك عن عثمان الله وهو قول الكرخي والطحاوي؛ لأنه يعتمد على صحة العقل وهو زائل فصار كما لو زال عقله بدواء وبنج واختيار أبي الفضل الكرماني أن الفتوى عليه، وفي "العيون" يفتي بالوقوع زجراً له، ولو سكر من الأنبذة المتخذة من الحبوب أو العسل لا يقع طلاقه عند الشيخين، وعند محمد: يقع بناء على أنه حرام أم لا، وفي "المحيط" ذكر عبد العزيز الترمذي سألت أبا حنيفة وسفيان عن رجل شرب البنج فارتفع إلى رأسه فطلق امرأته قالا: إن كان حين شرب يعلم أنه ما هو تطلق امرأته، وإن لم يعلم لم تطلق. وفي "الغاية": وشرب البنج والدواء يكون غالباً للتداوي، فلا يكون زوال العقل بسبب المعصية وهو المعتبر في الإيقاع، ولهذا لو شرب الخمر أو النبيذ فأخذه الصداع فزال عقله بالصداع لا بالسكر لا يقع طلاقه؛ لأن زوال العقل لم يحصل بسبب هو معصية. رمز الحقائق ٢٣٤/١٢] ومحل طلاق سكران إذا كان أصيلا، أما إذا وكله رجل بطلاق فطلق وهو سكران لا يقع إن كان وكيلا بالطلاق على مال ولو بلا مال وقع. (فتح) وأخرس بإشارته [عطف على قوله: "سكران" أي ولو كان الزوج أخرس يقع طلاقه. (ع)] أي يقع طلاقه بإشارته وكذا جميع تصرفاته كإعتاقه وبيعه وشرائه ،وغيرها وهذا إذا ولد أخرس أو طرأ عليه ودام إلى الموت، وعليه الفتوى وإن لم يدم لا يقع طلاقه، وقال بعض الشافعية: إنه إن كان يحسن الكتابة لم يقع طلاقه بدونها وهو قول حسن. (فتح، عيني) بإشارته المعهود إذا كانت تعرف لحاجته إليه. (ط،ع) حراً: أي حراً كان الزوج أو عبداً ولو مدبرا أو مكاتبا. (ط) أو عبداً: إنما وقع طلاق العبد على امرأته دون طلاق مولاه؛ لقول ابن عباس لما جاء النبي عليه رجل، فقال: أمته یا رسول الله ! سيدي زوجني وهو يريد أن يفرق بيني وبينها فصعد النبي عليهة المنبر فقال: "يا أيها الناس! ما بال أحدكم يزوج عبده من أمته يريد أن يفرق بينهما إنما الطلاق لمن أخذ بالساق، رواه ابن ماجه من رواية ابن لهيعة، وفيه مقال، ورواه الدار قطني . من غيره. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٥/١] لا طلاق الصبي والمجنون ولو مراهقاً أو أجازه بعد البلوغ. (ط) كما مر من قوله عليه: "كل طلاق جائز إلا طلاق الصبي والمجنون"، وقوله : " والنائم" وعدم جواز طلاقه ؛ لانتفاء الإرادة، ولهذا لا يتصف بصدق ولا كذب، ولا خبر ولا إنشاء، وقوله : والسيد" على امرأة "عبده" سواء كان كامل الملك أولا؛ لما مر من حديث ابن عباس ما؛ ولأن ملك النكاح حق العبد، فيكون الإسقاط إليه دون المولى. (فتح)
And a drunkard, and a deaf-mute by his gesture, free or slave; there is no divorce of the boy, the insane, and the sleeper; and the master [i.e., his divorce does not occur] on the wife of his slave, even if she is the property of the master. Drunkard [conjunction to his statement 'even if coerced' meaning even if the husband is drunk. (Ayni)] According to al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and Ahmad in one narration, his divorce does not occur. That has been transmitted from Uthman ibn Affan (رضي الله عنه), and it is the view of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi because it depends on sound intellect which is gone, so it becomes like one whose intellect has vanished due to medicine or bhang. The choice of Abu al-Fadl al-Kirmani is that the fatwa is upon it. In al-Uyun, he gives fatwa of occurrence as a deterrent. If he becomes drunk from beverages made from grains or honey, his divorce does not occur according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and according to Muhammad it occurs based on whether it is haram or not. In al-Muhit, it is mentioned that Abd al-Aziz al-Tirmidhi said: I asked Abu Hanifa and Sufyan about a man who drank bhang and it rose to his head, then he divorced his wife. They said: If at the time of drinking he knew what it was, his wife is divorced; if he did not know, she is not divorced. In al-Ghayah: Drinking bhang and medicine is usually for treatment, so loss of intellect due to disobedience is what is considered in occurrence; for this reason, if he drank wine or nabidh and a headache overtook him and his intellect left due to headache not intoxication, his divorce does not occur because the loss of intellect did not happen due to a cause that is disobedience. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/234] The application of a drunk's divorce is when he is the principal; but if a man appointed him as agent for divorce and he divorces while drunk, it does not occur if he was an agent for divorce for compensation, but even without compensation it occurs. (Fath) And a deaf-mute by his gesture [conjunction to his statement 'drunkard' meaning even if the husband is deaf-mute, his divorce occurs. (Ayni)] i.e., his divorce occurs by his gesture, and likewise all his transactions like manumission, sale, purchase, etc. This applies if he was born deaf-mute or it occurred later and persisted until death. Upon it is the fatwa. If it did not persist, his divorce does not occur. Some Shafi'is said: If he knows how to write, his divorce does not occur without it, which is a good opinion. (Fath, Ayni) By his known gesture if it is recognized due to his need. (Tatawkhani, Ayni) Free: i.e., whether the husband is free or a slave, even mudabbar or mukatab. (Tatawkhani) Or slave: Indeed the divorce of a slave on his wife occurs, not the divorce of his master; due to the statement of Ibn Abbas: When a man came to the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) and said: O Messenger of Allah! My master married me to his slave girl and now he wants to separate me from her. So the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ascended the pulpit and said: 'O people! What is the matter with one of you who marries his slave to his slave girl and then wants to separate them? Indeed divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg.' Narrated by Ibn Majah from Ibn Lahi'a, which has some criticism, and al-Daraqutni narrated it from another chain. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/235] There is no divorce of a boy and an insane person, even if he is near puberty or if he allowed it after puberty. (Tatawkhani) As previously from his statement: 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a boy and an insane person.' And his statement: 'And the sleeper' and the non-permissibility of his divorce due to lack of will; therefore it is not described as truth or falsehood, nor as informative or performative. And his statement: 'And the master' on the wife of 'his slave', whether he has complete ownership or not; due to the hadith of Ibn Abbas previously, and because the right of marriage belongs to the slave, so its termination is to him, not to the master. (Fath)
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage covers three special cases. First, the drunkard (sakran): Hanafi school holds his divorce valid, though some Hanafis (like al-Karkhi) disagreed. The fatwa for deterrent purposes is that it occurs. Differences exist based on intoxicant type: if from grains/honey, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say invalid, Muhammad says valid depending on prohibition. Second, the deaf-mute (akhras): his divorce by gesture is valid if the gesture is understood, and if he was born deaf-mute or it persisted until death. Third, the master cannot divorce his slave's wife; only the slave can. The text also reiterates that divorce of a boy, insane, or sleeper is invalid due to lack of will. The hadith 'divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg' (the husband) is cited for the master's inability.

Key Terms
سكران (sakran): One who has lost intellect due to intoxication; his divorce is valid per Hanafi.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a divorce uttered by a drunk person?
A) It is invalid because the drunk lacks intellect.
B) It is valid only if the drink was wine, not grain alcohol.
C) It is valid as a deterrent.
D) It is invalid if the drunk was an agent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a drunkard's divorce is valid as a deterrent, even though he lacks full intent, reflecting the principle that one cannot use self-induced intoxication to escape legal consequences.

Divorce by Deaf-Mute and Master's Divorce on Slave's Wife
Two rulings: one with conditions, one with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain when a deaf-mute's gesture constitutes a valid divorce and why a master cannot divorce his slave's wife.

Core Idea
A deaf-mute's understood gesture divorces; a master has no authority over his slave's marriage.

This lesson covers two more special cases: the deaf-mute (akhras) who communicates by gesture, and the master's attempt to divorce his slave's wife. The first affirms that a deaf-mute's divorce is valid if his gesture is understood, under certain conditions. The second reaffirms the principle that marriage is the right of the husband, not the master.

Guided Path
1
State the rule for the deaf-mute
The passage begins by stating that a deaf-mute's divorce occurs by his gesture, just like his other transactions.
This applies whether the deaf-mute is free or slave.
2
Identify the conditions for validity
The gesture must be known (ma'hud) and understood due to need. The condition (arising) of the deafness must be either born deaf or it occurred and persisted until death.
If the deafness is temporary and not permanent, the divorce by gesture is not valid.
3
Note the Shafi'i objection
Some Shafi'is say that if the deaf-mute can write, the divorce must be in writing, not just gesture. This is mentioned as a 'good opinion' but not adopted in Hanafi fatwa.
The Hanafi position is that gesture suffices.
4
State the rule for the master's divorce
The master cannot divorce the wife of his male slave, even if the slave wife is also the master's property.
The hadith 'divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg' (the husband) is cited.
5
Understand the legal basis
The right to divorce belongs to the husband (the slave), not the master, because the marriage contract is between the slave and his wife. The master's ownership of the slave does not extend to control over the marriage.
The passage also mentions the hadith of Ibn Abbas.

Source Text

وسكران، وأخرس بإشارته حرّاً أو عبداً ، لا طلاق الصبي والمجنون والنائم، والسيد أي لا يقع على امرأة عبده، ولو مملوكة للسيد وسكران [عطف على قوله: "مكرها أي ولو كان الزوج سكران. (ع)] وعند الشافعي في قول، وأحمد في رواية: لا يقع طلاقه، وقد نقل ذلك عن عثمان الله وهو قول الكرخي والطحاوي؛ لأنه يعتمد على صحة العقل وهو زائل فصار كما لو زال عقله بدواء وبنج واختيار أبي الفضل الكرماني أن الفتوى عليه، وفي "العيون" يفتي بالوقوع زجراً له، ولو سكر من الأنبذة المتخذة من الحبوب أو العسل لا يقع طلاقه عند الشيخين، وعند محمد: يقع بناء على أنه حرام أم لا، وفي "المحيط" ذكر عبد العزيز الترمذي سألت أبا حنيفة وسفيان عن رجل شرب البنج فارتفع إلى رأسه فطلق امرأته قالا: إن كان حين شرب يعلم أنه ما هو تطلق امرأته، وإن لم يعلم لم تطلق. وفي "الغاية": وشرب البنج والدواء يكون غالباً للتداوي، فلا يكون زوال العقل بسبب المعصية وهو المعتبر في الإيقاع، ولهذا لو شرب الخمر أو النبيذ فأخذه الصداع فزال عقله بالصداع لا بالسكر لا يقع طلاقه؛ لأن زوال العقل لم يحصل بسبب هو معصية. رمز الحقائق ٢٣٤/١٢] ومحل طلاق سكران إذا كان أصيلا، أما إذا وكله رجل بطلاق فطلق وهو سكران لا يقع إن كان وكيلا بالطلاق على مال ولو بلا مال وقع. (فتح) وأخرس بإشارته [عطف على قوله: "سكران" أي ولو كان الزوج أخرس يقع طلاقه. (ع)] أي يقع طلاقه بإشارته وكذا جميع تصرفاته كإعتاقه وبيعه وشرائه ،وغيرها وهذا إذا ولد أخرس أو طرأ عليه ودام إلى الموت، وعليه الفتوى وإن لم يدم لا يقع طلاقه، وقال بعض الشافعية: إنه إن كان يحسن الكتابة لم يقع طلاقه بدونها وهو قول حسن. (فتح، عيني) بإشارته المعهود إذا كانت تعرف لحاجته إليه. (ط،ع) حراً: أي حراً كان الزوج أو عبداً ولو مدبرا أو مكاتبا. (ط) أو عبداً: إنما وقع طلاق العبد على امرأته دون طلاق مولاه؛ لقول ابن عباس لما جاء النبي عليه رجل، فقال: أمته یا رسول الله ! سيدي زوجني وهو يريد أن يفرق بيني وبينها فصعد النبي عليهة المنبر فقال: "يا أيها الناس! ما بال أحدكم يزوج عبده من أمته يريد أن يفرق بينهما إنما الطلاق لمن أخذ بالساق، رواه ابن ماجه من رواية ابن لهيعة، وفيه مقال، ورواه الدار قطني . من غيره. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٥/١] لا طلاق الصبي والمجنون ولو مراهقاً أو أجازه بعد البلوغ. (ط) كما مر من قوله عليه: "كل طلاق جائز إلا طلاق الصبي والمجنون"، وقوله : " والنائم" وعدم جواز طلاقه ؛ لانتفاء الإرادة، ولهذا لا يتصف بصدق ولا كذب، ولا خبر ولا إنشاء، وقوله : والسيد" على امرأة "عبده" سواء كان كامل الملك أولا؛ لما مر من حديث ابن عباس ما؛ ولأن ملك النكاح حق العبد، فيكون الإسقاط إليه دون المولى. (فتح)
And a drunkard, and a deaf-mute by his gesture, free or slave; there is no divorce of the boy, the insane, and the sleeper; and the master [i.e., his divorce does not occur] on the wife of his slave, even if she is the property of the master. Drunkard [conjunction to his statement 'even if coerced' meaning even if the husband is drunk. (Ayni)] According to al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and Ahmad in one narration, his divorce does not occur. That has been transmitted from Uthman ibn Affan (رضي الله عنه), and it is the view of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi because it depends on sound intellect which is gone, so it becomes like one whose intellect has vanished due to medicine or bhang. The choice of Abu al-Fadl al-Kirmani is that the fatwa is upon it. In al-Uyun, he gives fatwa of occurrence as a deterrent. If he becomes drunk from beverages made from grains or honey, his divorce does not occur according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), and according to Muhammad it occurs based on whether it is haram or not. In al-Muhit, it is mentioned that Abd al-Aziz al-Tirmidhi said: I asked Abu Hanifa and Sufyan about a man who drank bhang and it rose to his head, then he divorced his wife. They said: If at the time of drinking he knew what it was, his wife is divorced; if he did not know, she is not divorced. In al-Ghayah: Drinking bhang and medicine is usually for treatment, so loss of intellect due to disobedience is what is considered in occurrence; for this reason, if he drank wine or nabidh and a headache overtook him and his intellect left due to headache not intoxication, his divorce does not occur because the loss of intellect did not happen due to a cause that is disobedience. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/234] The application of a drunk's divorce is when he is the principal; but if a man appointed him as agent for divorce and he divorces while drunk, it does not occur if he was an agent for divorce for compensation, but even without compensation it occurs. (Fath) And a deaf-mute by his gesture [conjunction to his statement 'drunkard' meaning even if the husband is deaf-mute, his divorce occurs. (Ayni)] i.e., his divorce occurs by his gesture, and likewise all his transactions like manumission, sale, purchase, etc. This applies if he was born deaf-mute or it occurred later and persisted until death. Upon it is the fatwa. If it did not persist, his divorce does not occur. Some Shafi'is said: If he knows how to write, his divorce does not occur without it, which is a good opinion. (Fath, Ayni) By his known gesture if it is recognized due to his need. (Tatawkhani, Ayni) Free: i.e., whether the husband is free or a slave, even mudabbar or mukatab. (Tatawkhani) Or slave: Indeed the divorce of a slave on his wife occurs, not the divorce of his master; due to the statement of Ibn Abbas: When a man came to the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) and said: O Messenger of Allah! My master married me to his slave girl and now he wants to separate me from her. So the Prophet (صلى الله عليه وسلم) ascended the pulpit and said: 'O people! What is the matter with one of you who marries his slave to his slave girl and then wants to separate them? Indeed divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg.' Narrated by Ibn Majah from Ibn Lahi'a, which has some criticism, and al-Daraqutni narrated it from another chain. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/235] There is no divorce of a boy and an insane person, even if he is near puberty or if he allowed it after puberty. (Tatawkhani) As previously from his statement: 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a boy and an insane person.' And his statement: 'And the sleeper' and the non-permissibility of his divorce due to lack of will; therefore it is not described as truth or falsehood, nor as informative or performative. And his statement: 'And the master' on the wife of 'his slave', whether he has complete ownership or not; due to the hadith of Ibn Abbas previously, and because the right of marriage belongs to the slave, so its termination is to him, not to the master. (Fath)
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage covers three special cases. First, the drunkard (sakran): Hanafi school holds his divorce valid, though some Hanafis (like al-Karkhi) disagreed. The fatwa for deterrent purposes is that it occurs. Differences exist based on intoxicant type: if from grains/honey, Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say invalid, Muhammad says valid depending on prohibition. Second, the deaf-mute (akhras): his divorce by gesture is valid if the gesture is understood, and if he was born deaf-mute or it persisted until death. Third, the master cannot divorce his slave's wife; only the slave can. The text also reiterates that divorce of a boy, insane, or sleeper is invalid due to lack of will. The hadith 'divorce belongs to the one who takes hold of the leg' (the husband) is cited for the master's inability.

Key Terms
أخرس (akhras): A deaf-mute person who communicates by gesture; his divorce by gesture is valid.
السيد على امرأة عبده (al-sayyid 'ala imra'at 'abdihi): The master's divorce of his slave's wife; it does not occur.

Checkpoint

A) If the gesture is not understood by his wife.
B) If the deaf-mute is literate.
C) If the deafness is not permanent (did not persist until death).
D) Both A and C.

Key Takeaway
A deaf-mute's divorce by a clear gesture is valid; a master has no power over his slave's marriage, affirming that the right to divorce belongs exclusively to the husband.

Divorce by an Intoxicated Person: The General Rule
Ruling on validity of divorce from an intoxicated husband

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by an intoxicated person and distinguish when it does and does not occur based on the cause of intoxication.

Core Idea
Divorce from prohibited intoxication is valid; from non-prohibited causes, it is not.

The Hanafi school holds that an intoxicated person is responsible for his actions if the intoxication resulted from a sinful act. This lesson unpacks the general rule: divorce by an intoxicated person is valid as a deterrent, but only if the loss of intellect was due to a prohibited intoxicant. We will also see the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the scope of this rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general Hanafi position
From the passage, extract the mufta bihi ruling: divorce by an intoxicated person is valid (falls). Note the reason: as a deterrent (zajran).
The deterrence rationale is key – it ties responsibility to the sinful act.
2
Examine the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad (in one narration) hold that divorce does not occur because the mind is absent. Their analogy: like medicine or narcotic that removes sanity.
The Hanafi response distinguishes between sinful and non-sinful causes.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say: if intoxication is from grains or honey (non-prohibited), divorce does not occur. Muhammad says: it depends on whether the drink is prohibited.
The mufta bihi position: fatwa on occurrence for prohibited intoxicants, non-occurrence for non-prohibited.
4
Apply the condition of sinful cause
From the examples: drinking wine then headache, or drinking banj unknowingly – no divorce because loss of intellect was not due to sin.
The principle: 'zawal al-aql bi-sabab huwa ma'siya' (loss of intellect due to a sinful cause) is the trigger.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسكران [عطف على قوله: "مكرها أي ولو كان الزوج سكران. (ع)] وعند الشافعي في قول، وأحمد في رواية: لا يقع طلاقه، وقد نقل ذلك عن عثمان الله وهو قول الكرخي والطحاوي؛ لأنه يعتمد على صحة العقل وهو زائل فصار كما لو زال عقله بدواء وبنج واختيار أبي الفضل الكرماني أن الفتوى عليه، وفي "العيون" يفتي بالوقوع زجراً له، ولو سكر من الأنبذة المتخذة من الحبوب أو العسل لا يقع طلاقه عند الشيخين، وعند محمد: يقع بناء على أنه حرام أم لا، وفي "المحيط" ذكر عبد العزيز الترمذي سألت أبا حنيفة وسفيان عن رجل شرب البنج فارتفع إلى رأسه فطلق امرأته قالا: إن كان حين شرب يعلم أنه ما هو تطلق امرأته، وإن لم يعلم لم تطلق. وفي "الغاية": وشرب البنج والدواء يكون غالباً للتداوي، فلا يكون زوال العقل بسبب المعصية وهو المعتبر في الإيقاع، ولهذا لو شرب الخمر أو النبيذ فأخذه الصداع فزال عقله بالصداع لا بالسكر لا يقع طلاقه؛ لأن زوال العقل لم يحصل بسبب هو معصية. رمز الحقائق ٢٣٤/١٢] ومحل طلاق سكران إذا كان أصيلا، أما إذا وكله رجل بطلاق فطلق وهو سكران لا يقع إن كان وكيلا بالطلاق على مال ولو بلا مال وقع. (فتح)
And an intoxicated person: [following the phrase: 'coerced'] meaning even if the husband was intoxicated. According to al-Shafi'i in one opinion, and Ahmad in one narration: his divorce does not occur, and this is attributed to Uthman, and it is the saying of al-Karkhi and al-Tahawi; because it [the ruling] depends on soundness of mind, which is absent, so it becomes like one whose mind is removed by medicine or narcotic. The choice of Abi al-Fadl al-Karmani is that the fatwa is upon it [i.e., non-occurrence]. And in al-'Uyun: fatwa is given that it occurs as a deterrent. If he became intoxicated from beverages made from grains or honey, his divorce does not occur according to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf); according to Muhammad, it occurs based on whether it is prohibited or not. And in al-Muhit: Abd al-Aziz al-Tirmidhi said: I asked Abu Hanifa and Sufyan about a man who drank banj (a narcotic) and it rose to his head and he divorced his wife; they said: If when he drank he knew what it was, his wife is divorced; if he did not know, she is not divorced. And in al-Ghayyah: Drinking banj and medicine is usually for treatment, so the loss of intellect is not due to sin, and that is the consideration in causing [divorce]. For this reason, if he drank wine or nabidh and was struck by a headache and lost his intellect due to the headache, not the intoxication, his divorce does not occur, because the loss of intellect did not occur due to a sinful cause. And the place for divorce of an intoxicated person is when he is the principal; if a man appointed him to divorce and he divorced while intoxicated, it does not occur if he was an agent for divorce in exchange for money; if without money, it occurs.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling on the validity of divorce pronounced by an intoxicated person. The Hanafi school generally holds that the divorce of an intoxicated person is valid (falling) as a deterrent and because the person is responsible due to the sinful cause of intoxication. However, this is qualified: if the intoxication is from a non-prohibited substance such as certain medicinal drinks or unintentionally, the divorce does not occur. The text also discusses the opinion of the Shafi'i and Hanbali schools, which generally do not consider it valid. The Hanafi position is based on the principle that the loss of intellect must be due to a sinful act for the divorce to take effect. The passage also distinguishes between the principal (the husband himself) and an agent; if an agent appointed to divorce for a fee becomes intoxicated, his divorce does not count; if appointed without fee, it does.

Key Terms
سكران (sukran): A person who has lost their intellect due to consuming an intoxicant.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, under what condition does the divorce of an intoxicated person NOT occur?
A) When intoxicated by wine
B) When intoxicated by narcotics like banj
C) When intoxicated by beverages from grains or honey
D) When intoxicated intentionally

Key Takeaway
Divorce by an intoxicated person is valid only if the loss of intellect was due to a sinful cause; otherwise it does not occur.

Divorce by an Intoxicated Agent: Nuances of Agency
Ruling on divorce by an intoxicated agent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish whether an intoxicated agent's divorce counts based on the type of agency (with or without payment).

Core Idea
An intoxicated agent's divorce does not count if he was appointed for money; it counts if appointed without money.

The passage then turns from the husband himself (asil) to an agent (wakil) appointed to divorce. The ruling changes when the agent becomes intoxicated and pronounces divorce. This lesson unpacks the special rule: the divorce does not take effect if the agency was for a fee (i.e., the agent was hired to divorce), but it does take effect if the agency was without payment (gratuitous). This distinction is based on the nature of the contract and the element of liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principal vs. agent distinction
The passage says: 'the place for divorce of an intoxicated person is when he is the principal (asil).' This means the general rule applies only to the husband himself.
The agent case is an exception, not a separate ruling.
2
Understand the two types of agency
Agency 'ala mal' (for money) vs. 'bila mal' (without money). In the first, the agent is hired; in the second, he acts voluntarily.
This affects liability and validity of the intoxicated act.
3
Apply the ruling to each type
If the agent was appointed to divorce in exchange for compensation (e.g., he is paid a fee for each divorce), his divorce while intoxicated does not occur. If the agency was unconditional and without payment, the divorce does occur.
Rationale: in paid agency, the agent is under contractual obligation; intoxication breaches the trust. In gratuitous agency, the agent is considered a volunteer, so his actions are closer to the principal's.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Divorce by a Mute Person: Validity and Conditions
Ruling on validity of divorce by a mute person using gestures

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce by a mute person and identify the two key conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
A mute person's divorce by a clear, customary gesture is valid only if the muteness is permanent until death.

Islamic law accommodates those unable to speak by allowing gestures to substitute for speech. This lesson examines the Hanafi position that a mute person's divorce is valid through an understood gesture, but only if the muteness is congenital or permanent until death. The ruling extends to all legal transactions, and we contrast it with a Shafi'i view that may require writing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Start by stating the core ruling: a mute person's divorce (and all legal actions) is valid using a clear gesture.
This is the mufta bihi Hanafi position.
2
Scope of the ruling
Note that the same principle applies to all transactions: manumission, sale, purchase, etc.
The text explicitly expands the rule.
3
First condition: permanence of muteness
The muteness must be congenital (born mute) or acquired but permanent until death. Temporary muteness does not allow divorce by gesture.
This is a key condition; without it the divorce does not occur.
4
Second condition: customary understood gesture
The gesture must be the one the mute person customarily uses and that others understand due to his need.
A random or unclear gesture is insufficient.
5
Contrast with Shafi'i view
Some Shafi'is add a condition: if the mute person can write, divorce only occurs through writing. Note that the Hanafi school does not adopt this view.
The text calls the Shafi'i opinion 'good' but fatwa is on the Hanafi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخرس بإشارته [عطف على قوله: "سكران" أي ولو كان الزوج أخرس يقع طلاقه. (ع)] أي يقع طلاقه بإشارته وكذا جميع تصرفاته كإعتاقه وبيعه وشرائه ،وغيرها وهذا إذا ولد أخرس أو طرأ عليه ودام إلى الموت، وعليه الفتوى وإن لم يدم لا يقع ،طلاقه، وقال بعض الشافعية: إنه إن كان يحسن الكتابة لم يقع طلاقه بدونها وهو قول حسن. And a mute person by his gesture: [following the phrase: 'intoxicated' meaning even if the husband was mute, his divorce occurs.] That is, his divorce occurs by his gesture, and likewise all his actions such as manumission, sale, purchase, etc. This is if he was born mute or it occurred suddenly and continued until death, and upon this is the fatwa. If it did not persist, his divorce does not occur. Some Shafi'is said that if he can write well, his divorce does not occur without writing, and this is a good opinion. By his customary gesture that is known due to his need for it.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage states the Hanafi ruling on divorce by a mute person. The divorce is valid if the mute person uses a clear, understood gesture indicating divorce. This applies to all legal transactions of a mute person. However, this only applies if the muteness is congenital or permanent (until death). If the muteness is temporary, divorce by gesture is not valid. The passage also notes a Shafi'i opinion that if the mute person can write, the gesture is insufficient without writing, which the text calls a 'good opinion' but not the Hanafi mufta bihi.

Key Terms
أخرس (akhras): A person who cannot speak due to a congenital or permanent condition.

Key Takeaway

Invalid Divorce: Minors, Insane, Sleepers, and Masters
Ruling that a master cannot divorce his slave's wife, with other invalid divorces.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four categories of persons whose divorce is invalid in Hanafi fiqh and explain the legal basis for each.

Core Idea
A valid divorce requires legal capacity and intention; minors, insane, sleeping, and masters over slaves' wives cannot divorce.

Divorce in Islamic law is a serious act with specific conditions. The Hanafi school identifies certain individuals whose divorce is inherently invalid due to lack of capacity or intention. This lesson examines four such cases from the classical text Kanz al-Daqaiq, supported by hadith and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
List the persons mentioned: a minor, an insane person, a sleeping person, and a master on behalf of his slave's wife.
The text also mentions a minor near puberty and ratification after puberty—both still invalid.
2
Understand the primary proof
Examine the hadith 'Every divorce is permissible except the divorce of a minor and an insane person.' Note that this is an explicit textual exception.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted as proof.
3
Analyze the reasoning for the sleeper
The sleeper's divorce is invalid due to absence of intention (iraada). A sleeping person cannot be truthful or lying, nor can they perform speech acts like informing or creating.
Intention is a fundamental condition for valid talaq.
4
Understand the master's position
The master cannot divorce his slave's wife because the marriage contract belongs to the slave; the right to divorce is with the husband (the slave), not the master.
This applies whether the master has full ownership or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا طلاق الصبي والمجنون ولو مراهقاً أو أجازه بعد البلوغ. (ط) كما مر من قوله عليه: "كل طلاق جائز إلا طلاق الصبي والمجنون ، وقوله : " والنائم" وعدم جواز طلاقه ؛ لانتفاء الإرادة، ولهذا لا يتصف بصدق ولا كذب، ولا خبر ولا إنشاء، وقوله : والسيد" على امرأة "عبده" سواء كان كامل الملك أولا؛ لما مر من حديث ابن عباس ما؛ ولأن ملك النكاح حق العبد، فيكون الإسقاط إليه دون المولى. And his saying: 'and the sleeper.' And the non-permissibility of his divorce due to the absence of intention, and for this reason he is not described as truthful or lying, nor as informer or creator. And his saying: 'and the master' regarding the wife of his slave, whether he has full ownership or not, because of what has passed from the hadith of Ibn Abbas, and because the right of marriage belongs to the slave, so the cancellation is up to him, not the master.
Source pages p. 401

Commentary
This passage covers several rulings: (1) A minor (even one close to puberty) cannot divorce, even if he later ratifies it, due to lack of legal capacity. (2) An insane person's divorce is invalid. (3) A sleeping person's divorce is invalid. (4) A master cannot divorce his slave's wife, because the marriage contract belongs to the slave; divorce right is with the husband (the slave). The hadith of Ibn Abbas is cited as proof that the master has no authority over the slave's marriage or divorce.

Key Terms
الصبي (al-sabi): A minor who has not reached puberty.

Key Takeaway
Divorce is invalid when issued by a minor, insane, sleeping person, or a master on behalf of his slave, due to lack of legal capacity or authority.

Number of Divorces: Free vs Slave Woman
Ruling that the number of divorces depends on the wife's status, not the husband's

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the number of divorces for free and slave women, identify the opposing school's position, and evaluate the proofs for each side.

Core Idea
A woman's divorces depend on her own status, not her husband's.

In this lesson, we examine a key difference between the Hanafi school and other schools: does the number of divorces a woman may receive depend on her own freedom or her husband's? The Hanafi school, based on a direct hadith and legal reasoning, rules that a free woman gets three divorces and a slave woman gets two, regardless of the husband's status. Let's explore the proof and the counter-argument.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core ruling: the number of divorces (and waiting period) is determined by the wife's status (free or slave).
Free woman: three divorces; slave woman: two divorces.
2
Identify the opposing school and their proof
The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools hold that the number depends on the husband's status. Their proof is the statement: 'Divorce is by the men, and the waiting period is by the women.'
They argue that since the husband pronounces divorce, his status should govern the count.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school relies on the hadith of Aisha: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menses.' This directly links the number to the woman's status.
The hadith is narrated by Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah.
4
Trace the Hanafi rebuttal of the opposing proof
The Hanafis reinterpret the opposing proof: 'Divorce is by men' means the pronouncing of divorce is done by men, not that the count is based on them. Additionally, they argue that divorce is a dissolution of marriage, a blessing in the woman's right, so it is halved for a slave woman.
The opposing proof is not taken as marfu' (elevated to Prophet) and is interpreted accordingly.
5
Note the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Apply the ruling: if a free woman marries a slave, Hanafi says she gets three divorces; Shafi'i says she gets two. If a slave woman marries a free man, Hanafi says she gets two divorces; Shafi'i says she gets three.
This difference affects actual divorce cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعتباره بالنساء، فطلاق الحرّة ثلاث والأمة ثنتان. واعتباره بالنساء [ أي اعتبار عدد الطلاق وكذا العدة. (ط)] وقال الشافعي: يعتبر الطلاق بحال الرجل؛ لقوله علي الطلاق بالرجال والعدة بالنساء وبه قال مالك وأحمد ولنا ما روته عائشة عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: "طلاق الأمة ثنتان وعدتها حيضتان ويروى ،قرءان رواه أبو داود والترمذي وابن ماجه، وقال الترمذي: حديث غريب؛ ولأن حل المحلية نعمة في حقها فيتنصف بالرق، وما رواه موقوف على ابن عباس، ذكره، أبو الفرج، وقيل: إنه كلام زيد بن ثابت الله ولم يثبت مرفوعاً، ولئن ثبت فمعناه أن إيقاعه بالرجال دون عدده. رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٥/١] (مستخلص) ثلاث: أي ثلاث طلقات وعدتها ثلاث حيض أو ثلاثة أشهر سواء كان زوجها حراً أو عبداً. (ط) والأمة أي وطلاق الأمة ولو مدبرة أو مكاتبة. (ط) ثنتان: أي طلقتان وعدتها حيضتان أو شهر ونصف سواء كانت تحت عبد أو حر. (ط)
And its consideration is by the women: so the divorce of a free woman is three, and of a slave woman is two. And its consideration is by the women [i.e., the number of divorces and likewise the waiting period]. Al-Shafi'i said: divorce is considered by the state of the husband, because of his saying: 'Divorce is by the men, and the waiting period is by the women.' And Malik and Ahmad also said this. And our proof is what Aisha narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he said: 'The divorce of a slave woman is two, and her waiting period is two menses' and it is also narrated as 'two menstrual cycles' — Abu Dawud, al-Tirmidhi, and Ibn Majah narrated it, and al-Tirmidhi said it is a strange hadith. And because the dissolution of marriage is a blessing in her right, so it is halved by slavery. And what is narrated as a statement of Ibn Abbas is mentioned by Abu al-Faraj, and it is said that it is the speech of Zayd ibn Thabit and it is not established as marfu'. And even if it is established, its meaning is that the pronouncing of divorce is by men, not its number. Three: i.e., three divorces, and her waiting period is three menses or three months, whether her husband is free or slave. And the slave woman: i.e., the divorce of a slave woman, even if she is mudabbara or mukataba. Two: i.e., two divorces, and her waiting period is two menses or one and a half months, whether she is under a slave or a free man.
Source pages p. 402

Commentary
This passage presents a key ruling: the number of divorces a woman may receive depends on her own status (free or slave), not on the status of her husband. A free woman can be divorced three times; a slave woman only twice. This applies regardless of whether her husband is free or slave. The Hanafi school argues this based on the hadith from Aisha directly stating the divorce of a slave woman is two. The Shafi'i, Maliki, and Hanbali schools hold that the number of divorces depends on the husband's status, because divorce is an act of the husband. The Hanafi school refutes the opposing proof by explaining that the statement 'divorce is by men' refers to who pronounces it, not the count. The text also notes that the same difference applies to the waiting period (idda): free woman: three menstrual cycles or three months; slave woman: two cycles or one and a half months.

Key Terms
الحرّة (al-hurrah): A free woman (not a slave)
الأمة (al-ammah): A slave woman (including mudabbara and mukataba)

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) One
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the number of divorces a woman may receive is determined solely by her own status (free or slave), not by her husband's status.

Explicit Divorce (Talaq Sarih) and Its Rulings
Definition and ruling of explicit divorce, fall as one raj'i

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of explicit divorce, identify its examples, and apply the Hanafi ruling that it falls as one revocable divorce regardless of intention.

Core Idea
In explicit divorce, the words alone effect one revocable divorce; intention for more is irrelevant.

This lesson focuses on the category of explicit divorce (talaq sarih) in Hanafi fiqh. We will define what makes a divorce pronouncement 'explicit', examine the key example 'Anti taliq', and understand the ruling that it results in a single revocable divorce even if the husband intends more. We will also explore the proof from the Quran and the scholarly dispute with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Define explicit divorce
Understand the definition: words whose meaning is so clear that they effect divorce without need for intention. Distinguish from allusive words (kinaayah).
The text provides both usuli and fiqhi definitions.
2
Identify examples of explicit words
List the primary example 'Anti taliq' and its variations (e.g., with fatha on lam, mispronunciations). Also note that addressing by name or 'my wife' counts.
Words like 'mutallaqa' (with sukun) are not explicit.
3
State the Hanafi ruling and its proof
Affirm that such words cause one revocable divorce even if the husband intends more. The proof is Quran 2:229 and the principle that explicit words do not bear multiple divorces.
The ruling is mufta bihi.
4
Analyze the dispute with opposing schools
Consider the arguments of Zufar, Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad who allow intention to increase the number. Understand the Hanafi response: the word is a report (khabar) and its legal implication is satisfied by one.
This dispute has significant practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الطلاق الصريح
أي في بيان أحكامه
ك دزف
هو كانت طالق ومطلقة وطلّقتك فيقع واحدةً رجعيّة وإن نوى الأكثر
أي وأنت
بهذه الألفاظ طلقة
متصلة المطلق من واحدة
باب الطلاق الصريح هو ما ظهر المراد منه ظهوراً بيناً حتى صار مكشوف المراد، ومنه سمي القصر صرحاً لظهوره. (ع)] لما فرغ من بيان أصل الطلاق ووصفه شرع في بيان تنويعه أي تنويع ما به الإيقاع من الصريح والكناية، فبدأ أولاً ببيان الصريح، وهو اسم لكل كلام مكشوف المراد كشفاً لا شبهة فيه بحيث يسبق إلى فهم السامع بمجرد السماع حقيقة كان أو مجازاً مراده، هذا عند الأصوليين، وعند الفقهاء: ما استعمل في الطلاق دون غيره وهو ما ثبت حكمه الشرعي بلا نية ولا يفتقر إلى إضمار أو تأويل، والصريح إنما يكون عند كثرة الاستعمال في المراد، ولا يشترط في كونه صريحاً عدم استعماله في غيره حتى لو استعمل في غيره نادراً لم يقدح
في كونه صريحاً. (فتح)
هو كأنت طالق [ أي الطلاق الصريح كقوله لامرأته أنت إلخ. (ع)] ولو بفتح اللام، وكذا لو أبدل الطاء تاء، أو القاف عيناً أو غيناً أو كافاً أو لاماً، ولم يصدق أنه لم يرد الطلاق إلا إذا أشهد على ذلك قبل التكلم سواء كان عالماً أو جاهلا وعليه الفتوى والتقييد بالخطاب اتفاقي بل لو قال زوجتي طالق، أو هي * طالق فهو مثل أنت طالق، والحاصل أن خصوص الخطاب ليس مراداً بل هو الأعم منه أو ما يقوم مقامه كالإضافة، وذكر اسمها الصريح، فإن تجردت عن ذلك لم تكن من الصريح، فيتوقف الوقوع حينئذ على النية. (فتح) واحدة رجعية: أي يقع بهذه الألفاظ واحدة رجعية؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿الطَّلاقُ مَرَّتَانِ فَإِمْسَاكٌ بِمَعْرُوفٍ أَوْ تَسْرِيحٌ بِإِحْسَانٍ ﴾ (البقرة:٢٢٩) وقد قالوا: الإمساك بالمعروف هو الرجعة، والتسريح بإحسان تركها حتى تمضي عدتها، وقوله: وإن نوى الأكثر ؛ لأنه ظاهر المراد، فتعلق الحكم بعين الكلام، وقام مقام معناه فاستغنى عن النية، وبنيته الإبانة قصد تنجيز ما علقه الشارع بانقضاء العدة، فيلغو قصده، وكذا نية الثلاث تغيير لمقتضى اللفظ فيلغو، وقال زفر والشافعي ومالك وأحمد يقع ما نوى؛ لأنه محتمل لفظه، فإن ذكر الطالق ذكر للطلاق لغة كذكر العالم ذكر للعلم لغة، فصار كالتصريح به؛ ولهذا يصح التفسير به فصار كالبائن بل أولى؛ لأنه صريح، والبائن كناية عنه. ولنا: أنه نوى ما لا يحتمله لفظه فتلغو نيته؛ لأن قوله : أنت طالق خبر واقتضاؤه أن يكون صادقاً إن كان مطابقاً أو كاذباً إن لم يكن مطابقاً، وأما الوقوع من جهة الزوج فلا يقتضيه اللفظ لغة، وإنما ثبت بالشرع اقتضاء؛ كيلا يكون كاذباً، ولا عموم للمقتضى؛ لأن ثبوته بالضرورة وقد اندفعت بواحدة، فلا حاجة إلى أزيد منها، بخلاف البائن؛ لأن البينونة متنوعة إلى غليظة وخفيفة، فكان اللفظ صالحاً لهما فتعمل نيته، ولو قال: أنت مطلقة بسكون الطاء لا يكون طلاقاً إلا بالنية؛ لأن لفظ الإطلاق ليس بمختص بالنساء، وغير مستعمل في الطلاق عرفاً فلم يكن صريحاً فيحتاج فيه إلى النية. (فتح، عيني)
Chapter of Explicit Divorce. i.e., concerning the explanation of its rulings. [Like such:] He said: It is 'you are divorced' and 'divorced woman' and 'I divorce you' so it falls as one revocable [divorce] even if he intended more. i.e., 'and you' with these words [indicates] one divorce connected to the divorcer from one [source]. The chapter of explicit divorce: it is what its meaning appears clearly until it becomes uncovered in meaning, hence the palace is called 'sarh' due to its clarity. (Ayni) After finishing the explanation of the essence of divorce and its description, he began to explain its division, i.e., the division of the means of effecting [divorce] into explicit and allusive. So he first begins with explaining the explicit, and it is a name for every speech whose meaning is uncovered without any ambiguity such that it comes to the understanding of the listener upon merely hearing it, whether it is real or metaphorical in its intended meaning. This is according to the usuliyyun. According to the fuqaha: it is what is used for divorce exclusively, and it is that whose legal ruling is established without intention and does not require concealment or interpretation. And explicitness only occurs with frequent usage in the intended meaning, and it is not a condition for being explicit that it is not used in other [contexts], so even if it is used rarely in other contexts, it does not harm its being explicit. (Fath) He said: It is like 'anti taliq' [i.e., explicit divorce is like his saying to his wife 'anti' etc.] even if the lam is with fatha, and likewise if he replaces the ta with a ta, or the qaf with ayn, ghayn, kaf, or lam, and he is not believed that he did not intend divorce unless he bears witness to that before speaking, whether he was knowledgeable or ignorant, and upon it is the fatwa. And the restriction to direct address is incidental; rather, if he says 'my wife is taliq' or 'she is taliq', it is like 'anti taliq'. The summary is that the particular form of address is not intended, but rather the general [concept] or what stands in its place like the possessive, and mentioning her explicit name. If it is stripped of that, it is not from the explicit, so the occurrence then depends on intention. (Fath) One revocable: i.e., with these words there occurs one revocable divorce, based on His statement, the Exalted: 'Divorce is twice; then honorable retention or kindly release' (2:229). And they said: Honorable retention is the return (raj'a), and kindly release is leaving her until her waiting period ends. And his saying 'even if he intended more' because it is apparent in meaning, so the ruling is attached to the very words, and they stand in place of its meaning, so there is no need for intention. And if he intends separation (ibana), he intends to hasten what the Lawgiver has postponed to the end of the iddah, so his intention is invalid. Likewise, the intention of three is to change the implication of the words, so it is invalid. And Zufar, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad said: whatever he intends occurs, because his words are probable [of bearing it], for mentioning 'taliq' is a mention of divorce linguistically, just as mentioning 'world' is a mention of knowledge linguistically, so it becomes like an explicit statement with it; for this reason interpretation with it is valid, so it becomes like bain [irrevocable] rather more so, because it is explicit while bain is allusive. And our proof: he intends what his words do not bear, so his intention is invalid. Because his saying 'anti taliq' is a report, and it requires being truthful if it is in accordance [with reality] or false if not. As for the occurrence of divorce from the husband's side, the words do not require it linguistically; it is only established by a legal implication so that he would not be a liar. And the implication has no generality, because it is established by necessity and has been satisfied by one, so there is no need for more. This is unlike the bain [pronouncement] because the state of irrevocability (baynuna) varies into severe and light, so the words are suitable for both, thus his intention works. And if he says 'anti mutallaqa' with a sukun on the ta, it is not a divorce except with intention, because the word 'itlaq' is not specific to women, and it is not used idiomatically for divorce, so it is not explicit and thus requires intention. (Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on explicit divorce (talaq sarih) in Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines the term and states the ruling. 'Explicit divorce' refers to words whose meaning is so clear that no interpretation is needed; they effect divorce without any need for intention (niyyah). The prime example is 'Anti taliq' (You are divorced). Even if the husband intends more than one divorce, it falls as one revocable divorce. The proof is the Quranic verse (2:229) which indicates that each pronouncement counts as one. The text then engages in a dispute with Zufar, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, who hold that intention can increase the number. The Hanafi argument is that the word 'taliq' is a report (khabar) and cannot bear the meaning of multiple divorces; the legal effect is an implication to avoid lying, and that implication is satisfied by one. In contrast, for allusive words (kinaayah), intention does matter because the words are ambiguous. Also, mispronunciations or variants do not affect the explicitness, and the husband's claim that he did not intend divorce is not accepted unless he testified before speaking. The ruling also extends to phrases like 'my wife is taliq' or naming her explicitly. However, if one says 'mutallaqa' with sukun on the ta, it is not explicit and requires intention.

Key Terms
الطلاق الصريح (al-talaq al-sarih): Words whose meaning is so clear that they effect divorce without need for intention, e.g., 'Anti taliq'.
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The right to take back one's wife during the iddah after a revocable divorce (talaq raj'i).
الكناية (al-kinaayah): Allusive words that do not clearly indicate divorce and thus require intention to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his free wife 'Anti taliq' intending three divorces, how many divorces occur?
A) Three divorces, because his intention is clear.
B) One revocable divorce, because explicit words cannot bear more.
C) One irrevocable divorce, because intention for more makes it three.
D) None, because the intention conflicts with the words.

Key Takeaway
Explicit divorce words cause one revocable divorce regardless of intention; the husband's claim to the contrary is not accepted without prior testimony.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Ruling of the Explicit Pronouncement 'Anti Tāliq'
Defines explicit divorce and rules on 'anti tāliq' with dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on 'anti ṭāliq' and explain why intention for more than one divorce is disregarded, while comparing the opposing school's argument.

Core Idea
The explicit word 'anti ṭāliq' effects only one revocable divorce; intention for more is legally irrelevant.

This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on explicit divorce (ṭalāq ṣarīḥ). According to the school, certain words are so clear that they produce a divorce without any need for intention. The phrase 'anti ṭāliq' is the prime example. However, a dispute arises when the husband intends more than one divorce or an irrevocable type. The passage from Kanz al-Daqāʾiq explains the Hanafi position and the counter-arguments from Zufar, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad. Understanding this dispute is crucial for correctly applying the law in practice.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling that the explicit pronouncement 'anti ṭāliq' produces one revocable divorce regardless of the husband's intention.
This is based on the Quranic verse 2:229.
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that Zufar, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad hold that the husband's intention for the number or type of divorce is effective.
They argue the word 'ṭāliq' is a verbal noun and can be general.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Understand their linguistic argument: mentioning 'ṭāliq' is like mentioning knowledge, so it carries the meaning of divorce generally.
They compare it to explicit pronouncements that allow interpretation.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Explore the Hanafi rebuttal: the word is a report (khabar) that must be truthful; the law makes it effective only to the extent necessary to avoid falsehood, which is one divorce.
No generality is needed beyond necessity.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Appreciate the practical difference: under the opposing view, a man who intends three divorces would effect three; under the Hanafi view, only one revocable.
This significantly impacts marital status and possibility of reconciliation.

كتاب الطلاق
باب الطلاق الصريح
هو كانت طالق ومطلقة وطلّقتك فيقع واحدةً رجعيّة وإن نوى الأكثر
باب الطلاق الصريح هو ما ظهر المراد منه ظهوراً بيناً حتى صار مكشوف المراد، ومنه سمي القصر صرحاً لظهوره. (ع)] لما فرغ من بيان أصل الطلاق ووصفه شرع في بيان تنويعه أي تنويع ما به الإيقاع من الصريح والكناية، فبدأ أولاً ببيان الصريح، وهو اسم لكل كلام مكشوف المراد كشفاً لا شبهة فيه بحيث يسبق إلى فهم السامع بمجرد السماع حقيقة كان أو مجازاً مراده، هذا عند الأصوليين، وعند الفقهاء: ما استعمل في الطلاق دون غيره وهو ما ثبت حكمه الشرعي بلا نية ولا يفتقر إلى إضمار أو تأويل، والصريح إنما يكون عند كثرة الاستعمال في المراد، ولا يشترط في كونه صريحاً عدم استعماله في غيره حتى لو استعمل في غيره نادراً لم يقدح في كونه صريحاً. (فتح)
هو كأنت طالق [ أي الطلاق الصريح كقوله لامرأته أنت إلخ. (ع)] ولو بفتح اللام، وكذا لو أبدل الطاء تاء، أو القاف عيناً أو غيناً أو كافاً أو لاماً، ولم يصدق أنه لم يرد الطلاق إلا إذا أشهد على ذلك قبل التكلم سواء كان عالماً أو جاهلا وعليه الفتوى والتقييد بالخطاب اتفاقي بل لو قال زوجتي طالق، أو هي * طالق فهو مثل أنت طالق، والحاصل أن خصوص الخطاب ليس مراداً بل هو الأعم منه أو ما يقوم مقامه كالإضافة، وذكر اسمها الصريح، فإن تجردت عن ذلك لم تكن من الصريح، فيتوقف الوقوع حينئذ على النية. (فتح) واحدة رجعية: أي يقع بهذه الألفاظ واحدة رجعية؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿الطَّلاقُ مَرَّتَانِ فَإِمْسَاكٌ بِمَعْرُوفٍ أَوْ تَسْرِيحٌ بِإِحْسَانٍ ﴾ (البقرة:٢٢٩) وقد قالوا: الإمساك بالمعروف هو الرجعة، والتسريح بإحسان تركها حتى تمضي عدتها، وقوله: وإن نوى الأكثر ؛ لأنه ظاهر المراد، فتعلق الحكم بعين الكلام، وقام مقام معناه فاستغنى عن النية، وبنيته الإبانة قصد تنجيز ما علقه الشارع بانقضاء العدة، فيلغو قصده، وكذا نية الثلاث تغيير لمقتضى اللفظ فيلغو، وقال زفر والشافعي ومالك وأحمد يقع ما نوى؛ لأنه محتمل لفظه، فإن ذكر الطالق ذكر للطلاق لغة كذكر العالم ذكر للعلم لغة، فصار كالتصريح به؛ ولهذا يصح التفسير به فصار كالبائن بل أولى؛ لأنه صريح، والبائن كناية عنه. ولنا: أنه نوى ما لا يحتمله لفظه فتلغو نيته؛ لأن قوله : أنت طالق خبر واقتضاؤه أن يكون صادقاً إن كان مطابقاً أو كاذباً إن لم يكن مطابقاً، وأما الوقوع من جهة الزوج فلا يقتضيه اللفظ لغة، وإنما ثبت بالشرع اقتضاء؛ كيلا يكون كاذباً، ولا عموم للمقتضى؛ لأن ثبوته بالضرورة وقد اندفعت بواحدة، فلا حاجة إلى أزيد منها، بخلاف البائن؛ لأن البينونة متنوعة إلى غليظة وخفيفة، فكان اللفظ صالحاً لهما فتعمل نيته، ولو قال: أنت مطلقة بسكون الطاء لا يكون طلاقاً إلا بالنية؛ لأن لفظ الإطلاق ليس بمختص بالنساء، وغير مستعمل في الطلاق عرفاً فلم يكن صريحاً فيحتاج فيه إلى النية. (فتح، عيني)
The book of divorce. Chapter of explicit divorce. He said: 'You are divorced, and divorced, and I divorce you' so one revocable divorce occurs even if he intends more. The chapter of explicit divorce: it is that from which the intended meaning appears clearly until it becomes uncovered. So the term 'ṣarīḥ' is derived from 'palace' due to its clarity. (Ayni)] When he finished explaining the origin of divorce and its description, he began explaining its division, i.e., division of what effects divorce into explicit and implicit. He first began with the explicit, which is a name for every speech whose meaning is uncovered without ambiguity, such that it immediately comes to the mind of the listener upon hearing it, whether literal or metaphorical, according to the usuliyyun. According to the jurists: it is what is used for divorce specifically, and its legal ruling is established without intention and does not require implication or interpretation. Explicitness only occurs with frequent usage in the intended meaning, and it is not a condition for explicitness that it is not used for other things, so even if used rarely for other things, it does not harm its explicitness. (Fath). It is like 'You are divorced' [i.e., explicit divorce is like his statement to his wife 'You are divorced' etc. (Ayni)] even if the lam is opened, and likewise if he changes the ṭa to ta, or the qaf to 'ayn, ghayn, kaf, or lam, and he is not believed that he did not intend divorce unless he bears witness to that before speaking, whether he is knowledgeable or ignorant, and upon this is the fatwa. The restriction to addressing is incidental; rather, if he says 'my wife is divorced', or 'she is divorced', it is like 'you are divorced'. The conclusion is that specific address is not intended, but rather the general or what stands in its place like the possessive construction, and mentioning her explicit name. If it is devoid of that, it is not explicit, so occurrence then depends on intention. (Fath). One revocable: i.e., with these words one revocable divorce occurs, due to His statement: 'Divorce is twice, then honorable retention or release with kindness' (2:229). They said: honorable retention is return (raj'a), and release with kindness is leaving her until her waiting period ends. And His statement: 'even if he intends more' – because it is clear intent, so the ruling is attached to the very words, and they stand in place of meaning, so he is independent of intention. If he intends separation (ibānah), he intends to implement what the Lawgiver has attached to the end of the waiting period, so his intention is futile. Likewise, intention of three is a change from what the word requires, so it is futile. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad said: what he intended occurs, because his word is probable for it. For indeed mentioning 'the divorcing one' (al-ṭāliq) is mentioning divorce linguistically, like mentioning 'the knowledge' is mentioning knowledge linguistically, so it becomes like explicit pronouncement; therefore interpretation by it is correct, so it becomes like irrevocable (bā'in), nay even more so because it is explicit, while irrevocable is a metonymy for it. Our proof: he intended what his word does not bear, so his intention is futile; because his statement 'you are divorced' is a report, and its requirement is that it be true if conforming or false if not. As for occurrence from the husband's side, the word does not linguistically require it; it is only established by the Law as a requirement so that he not be a liar. There is no generality in the requirement because its establishment is by necessity, and necessity is fulfilled by one, so there is no need for more. Unlike the irrevocable (bā'in), because irrevocability varies into severe and light, so the word is suitable for both, so his intention takes effect. If he says 'anti muṭallaqah' with sukoon on the ṭa, it is not divorce except with intention, because the word 'iṭlāq' is not specific to women, and is not customarily used for divorce, so it is not explicit, thus it requires intention. (Fath, Ayni)
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqā'iq explains the chapter on explicit divorce (al-ṭalāq al-ṣarīḥ). The commentator begins by defining ṣarīḥ: words whose meaning is immediately clear to the listener, requiring no interpretation. According to the jurists, explicit divorce words are those used exclusively for divorce and take effect without intention. The prime example is 'anti ṭāliq' (you are divorced), even if pronounced with a slight phonetic variation. The same ruling applies if he says 'my wife is divorced' or 'she is divorced'. The key ruling is that these words produce one revocable divorce (ṭalāq rajʿī), based on Quran 2:229 which indicates that after one or two divorces, the husband may retain the wife during her ʿiddah. If the husband intends more than one divorce (e.g., three) or intends irrevocable separation (ibānah), his intention is disregarded because the word does not bear that meaning. The passage then presents the opposing view of Zufar, al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad, who argue that the husband's intention should take effect because the word is capable of it. The Hanafi response is that the word 'anti ṭāliq' is a report (khabar) that must be truthful; the law makes it effective as a divorce only to prevent falsehood. Since the requirement of truth is satisfied by one divorce, there is no need for more. The exception is the word 'bā'in' (irrevocable), which does allow for different degrees, so intention matters there. Finally, the passage notes that 'anti muṭallaqah' (with sukoon on ṭa) is not explicit because it is not customarily used for divorce and requires intention.

Key Terms
طلاق صريح (ṭalāq ṣarīḥ): A divorce pronouncement whose meaning is unequivocally clear, requiring no intention or interpretation to effect divorce.
واحدة رجعية (wāḥidah rajʿiyyah): A single revocable divorce, meaning the husband may take his wife back during her waiting period without a new marriage contract.
نية (niyyah): Intention in the heart regarding the number or type of divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his wife 'anti ṭāliq' intending three divorces, what is the result?
A) Three divorces occur.
B) One revocable divorce occurs.
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs.
D) No divorce occurs.

Key Takeaway
When a husband says 'anti ṭāliq' (or its clear equivalents), a single revocable divorce occurs regardless of his inner intention.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Explicit Divorce Phrases: Default Ruling and Linguistic Basis
Rulings for phrases like 'anti al-ṭalāq', 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq', and adding the woman's parts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default ruling when a husband says 'anti al-ṭalāq', 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq', or 'anti ṭāliq', and explain why the maṣdar ('al-ṭalāq') is treated as explicit.

Core Idea
Any word that clearly indicates divorce without intent is explicit; the source stands for the agent.

In this lesson, we examine additional divorce expressions classified as ṣarīḥ (explicit) by the Hanafi school. The key is that these words leave no room for ambiguity—they trigger divorce without needing to prove intent. We will also explore the linguistic reasoning behind treating the word 'al-ṭalāq' (the divorce) as equivalent to 'ṭāliq' (divorcing).

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit phrases listed
The passage lists three key phrases: 'anti al-ṭalāq', 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq', and 'anti ṭāliq'. These are all considered explicit (ṣarīḥ).
2
Understand the linguistic mechanism
For 'anti al-ṭalāq', the maṣdar (verbal noun) is used in place of the agent noun (ism fāʿil), similar to saying 'rajul ʿadl' for a just man. Therefore, it is equivalent to 'anti ṭāliq'.
3
Determine the default ruling
Without any intention, one revocable divorce occurs. The divorce is revocable because it is explicit and does not require intention.
4
Examine the effect of intention
If the husband intends one or two divorces, still only one occurs. If he intends three, three occur. Intention of two is disregarded for a free woman (to be detailed in later lesson).
5
Recognize other explicit constructions
Adding divorce to the woman's totality (her name) or to a representative part like neck, spirit, private parts, or body is also explicit because the part metaphorically stands for the whole.
Quranic evidence: 4:92, 26:4; prophetic hadith about private parts.

بهذه الألفاظ أي ثلاث تطليقات
