(مس) واحدة رجعية: أي تقع بهذه الصور الثلاث واحدة رجعية، أما الوقوع بقوله: "أنت الطلاق" فلأن المصدر يذكر ويراد به اسم الفاعل، يقال : رجل عدل أي عادل فيكون ذكر الطلاق ذكراً للطالق، فصار بمنزلة قوله: "أنت طالق"، وأما الوقوع بالأخريين فظاهر؛ لأنه لو ذكر أنت طالق وقع الطلاق، فإذا ذكره وذكر المصدر معه فالمصدر يزيده تأكيداً فكان أولى، وأما كون الواقع به رجعياً، فلأنه صريح لا يحتاج إلى النية. (مستخلص) واحدةً: أي طلقة واحدة لأنه حقيقة كلامه. (ع) أو ثنتين: أي إذا نوى في هذه الصور الثلاث طلقتين يكون واحدة أيضاً عندنا؛ لأن المصدر لا يحتمل العدد، وقال زفر والشافعي ومالك تصح نية الثنتين، لأنه إذا صح نية الثلاث تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأنهما بعض الثلاث، قلنا إن معنى التوحيد مراعى في الألفاظ الوحدان ورعاية معنى التوحيد إما بالفردية أو الجنسية، والمثنى بمعزل منهما؛ لكونه عدداً محضاً إلا أن تكون المرأة أمة؛ لأن ثنتين جنس طلاقها، فيكون معنى التوحيد مراعياً في نية الثنتين باعتبار الجنسية والحاصل أن المصدر لا يدل على العدد، وإنما يدل على الفرد الحقيقي أو الفرد الاعتباري والثلاث فرد اعتباري في الحرة؛ لكونه جنس طلاقها، وكذا الثنتان في الأمة، فثبت أن نية الثلاث جائزة بذكر المصدر دون نية الثنتين؛ لأنه عدد محض، فلفظ الجنس لا يدل عليه فتلغو نيته. (مستخلص، فتح) فثلاث: [ أي فتقع ثلاث؛ لأنها الطلاق لا من حيث العددية. (ع)] ولو قال: أنت طالق الطلاق، وقال: جنس عنيت بقولي: طالق" واحدة وبقولي "الطلاق" أخرى صدق؛ لأن كل واحدة منهما تصلح للإيقاع بإضمار أنت، فصار كقوله : "أنت طالق أنت "الطلاق فيقع رجعيتان إن كانت مدخولاً بها وإلا لغا الكلام الثاني. (فتح) وإن أضاف الطلاق الإضافة ههنا بمعنى التخصيص أي إذا خص الطلاق في جملة المرأة مثل قوله : "أنت طالق" فإن الإضافة فيه بطريق الحقيقة، وفيما يعبر عن الجملة الإضافة بطريق التجوز، ومن المجاز أيضاً الإيقاع بلفظ المصدر، وإنما يقع الطلاق في الإضافة إلى الجملة؛ لأن المرأة محله، وفيما يعبر به عن الجملة، فلأنها تذكر، ويراد به الجملة كالرقبة، فإنه يراد بها النفس كقوله تعالى: ﴿فَتَحْرِيرُ رَقَبَةٍ (النساء: ۹۲) وكذا العنق؛ لقوله تعالى: ﴿فَظَلَّتْ أَعْنَاقُهُمْ (الشعراء:٤) وكذا الروح يقال: "ملك روحه" أي نفسه، وكذا الفرج لقوله عليه: "لعن الله الفروج يركبن على السروج، وكون البدن والجسد عبارة عن الكل ظاهر. (مستخلص، فتح) إلى جملتها : أي جملة المرأة بأن قال لها : أنت طالق . (ط) :كالرقبة بأن قال: رقبتك طالق. (ع)
Words of explicit divorce: or separation, or he did not intend anything. If he says: 'You are the divorce' or 'You are divorced the divorce' or 'You are divorced' ... by mentioning the source (maṣdar) as definite for emphasis, one revocable divorce occurs without intention, or he intended one or two, and if he intended three, then three. The source (maṣdar) as indefinite for emphasis: i.e., one divorce, related to 'falls' by these words i.e., one divorce, even if he adds the divorce to her totality or to what expresses her like the neck. He said: or he intended separation. The husband to his wife. By mentioning the source definite for emphasis, one divorce occurs. The source indefinite for emphasis: i.e., one divorce. By these words i.e., three divorces. If he intended nothing: because the intent appeared, so the ruling attaches to the very words. If he says: 'anti muṭallaqah' with sukoon on the ṭa, it does not occur except with intention. (T, A). 'Anti al-ṭalāq': i.e., you are a divorce like a just man, or you are the possessor of divorce by deleting the construct (mus). One revocable: i.e., with these three forms one revocable divorce occurs. As for occurrence by his saying 'anti al-ṭalāq', because the source (maṣdar) is mentioned and the agent noun (ism al-fāʿil) is intended. It is said: a just man (rajul ʿadl) meaning just (ʿādil). So mentioning divorce becomes mentioning the divorcing one, so it becomes like his saying 'anti ṭāliq'. As for occurrence by the other two, it is apparent; because if he mentioned 'anti ṭāliq', divorce occurs, so if he mentions it and mentions the source with it, the source adds emphasis, so it is more appropriate. As for the occurrence being revocable, because it is explicit and does not need intention. (Mustakhlas) One: i.e., one divorce because it is the reality of his speech. (Ayni) Or two: i.e., if he intends in these three forms two divorces, it is also one according to us; because the source (maṣdar) does not bear number. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik said: intention of two is valid, because if intention of three is valid, then intention of two is valid, since two is part of three. We said: the meaning of unity (tawḥīd) is observed in words of singularity (wuḥdān). Observance of the meaning of unity is either by individuality (fardiyyah) or by genericness (jinsiyyah). The dual is far from both, being pure number. Except if the woman is a slave, because two is the generic number of her divorce, so the meaning of unity is observed in the intention of two by consideration of genericness. The conclusion is that the source does not indicate number; it indicates the real individual or the notional individual. And three is a notional individual for a free woman because it is the generic number of her divorce, and likewise two for a slave woman. So it is established that intention of three is permissible by mentioning the source, but not intention of two, because it is pure number, and the generic word does not indicate it, so his intention is futile. (Mustakhlas, Fath) So three: i.e., three occur, because it is the divorce not from the perspective of count. (Ayni) And if he says: 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq', and says: 'I meant by my words: ṭāliq one, and by 'al-ṭalāq' another,' he is believed, because each of them is suitable for effecting divorce by implying 'anti'. So it becomes like his saying: 'anti ṭāliq, anti al-ṭalāq', and two revocable divorces occur if she is consummated; otherwise the second speech is void. (Fath) And if he adds the divorce – the addition here means specification, i.e., if he specifies the divorce to the totality of the woman, like his saying 'anti ṭāliq', the addition is in reality. In what expresses the totality, the addition is metaphorical. And it is also metaphorical to effect divorce with the word of source (maṣdar). Divorce occurs in addition to the totality because the woman is its locus. In what expresses the totality, it is because she is mentioned and the totality is intended, like 'the neck' (al-raqabah), for it intends the self, as in His statement: 'then the freeing of a neck' (4:92). Likewise, 'the neck' (al-ʿunuq) as in His statement: 'so their necks became humbled' (26:4). Likewise, the spirit (al-rūḥ) – it is said: 'he owned his spirit' meaning his self. Likewise, the private parts (al-farj) due to his saying: 'May Allah curse the private parts that ride on saddles.' And that the body (al-badan, al-jasad) expresses the whole is apparent. (Mustakhlas, Fath). To her totality: i.e., the totality of the woman, such as his saying to her: 'anti ṭāliq'. (T) Like the neck: such as saying: 'your neck is divorced.' (Ayni)
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of explicit divorce words, listing additional phrases that fall under the ruling of ṣarīḥ. The first set includes seven forms: (1) 'anti al-ṭalāq' (you are the divorce), (2) 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq' (you are divorced the divorce), (3) 'anti ṭāliq' (you are divorced) – but the context clarifies these are all explicit. The ruling for all is one revocable divorce if no intention or intention of one or two, but if he intends three, three occur. The reasoning for 'anti al-ṭalāq' is that the source (maṣdar) is used to imply the agent noun (ism fāʿil), like saying 'rajul ʿadl' meaning 'just man'. So it is equivalent to 'anti ṭāliq'. For 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq', the repetition emphasizes, strengthening the occurrence. The passage then addresses the issue of intending two. The Hanafi position (following Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn) is that intention of two is ineffective; only one divorce occurs, because the source (maṣdar) does not indicate number. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik argue that if intention of three is valid, intention of two should also be valid since two is part of three. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the free woman, for whom three is the generic number (jins al-ṭalāq), and the slave woman, for whom two is the generic number. Thus, intention of three is valid for a free woman because the word indicates the generic (which is three), and intention of two is not because two is a pure number not indicated by the generic word. However, for a slave woman, intention of two is valid because two is her generic number. The passage also covers a special case: if he says 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq' and claims he meant one divorce by the first part and another by the second part (i.e., making two separate pronouncements), he is believed, and two revocable divorces occur if the marriage was consummated; if not consummated, the second part is superfluous. Finally, the passage discusses adding the divorce to the woman's totality (e.g., 'anti ṭāliq') or to a part that represents her whole, like 'neck', 'spirit', 'private parts', or 'body', using Quranic evidence and custom. All these are considered explicit because they intend the woman herself, and divorce occurs revocably without need for intention.

Key Terms
مصدر (maṣdar): Verbal noun; in this context, 'al-ṭalāq' (the divorce) used as a source to imply the agent 'divorcing one'.
فرد اعتباري (fard iʿtibārī): Notional individual; a single entity in the mind, such as three divorces being considered one generic unit (jins) for a free woman.
إضافة (iḍāfah): Construct state or addition; here, adding the word 'divorce' to the woman's name or a part representing her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the default number and type of divorce when a husband says 'anti al-ṭalāq' without any intention?
A) One revocable.
B) One irrevocable.
C) Three revocable.
D) None, requires intention.

Key Takeaway
A divorce is explicit (ṣarīḥ) if the wording itself clearly indicates divorce, making the pronouncement effective regardless of the husband's inner intention.

The Dispute on Intending Two Divorces and the Exception for Slave Women
Rulings for intending two divorces and the exception for slave women

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school rejects intention of two divorces from a free woman, justify the exception for slave women using the concept of fard iʿtibārī, and apply the ruling when a husband claims two separate pronouncements.

Core Idea
Two is pure number; the generic word indicates only unity or the generic (three for free, two for slave).

In this lesson, we delve into a key ikhtilāf: what happens when the husband uses one of the explicit phrases but intends two divorces. The Hanafi school maintains that only one divorce occurs, while Zufar, al-Shafiʿī, and Mālik argue for the validity of intending two. We will unpack the Hanafi rationale based on the nature of the maṣdar and the concept of notional individual (fard iʿtibārī).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on intending two
Intention of two is disregarded; only one divorce occurs.
2
Identify the opposing views
Zufar, al-Shafiʿī, and Mālik hold that intention of two is valid.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The maṣdar does not indicate number; it indicates the real individual or the notional individual. For a free woman, three is the notional individual (generic); for a slave woman, two is generic.
4
Apply the distinction between free and slave
Intention of three is valid for free; intention of two is valid for slave.
5
Examine the special case of 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq' with two separate intentions
If the husband claims he meant two separate pronouncements, he is believed, and two revocable divorces occur if consummated.
This is treated as two independent utterances, not one compound phrase.

'Anti al-ṭalāq': i.e., you are a divorce like a just man, or you are the possessor of divorce by deleting the construct (mus). One revocable: i.e., with these three forms one revocable divorce occurs. As for occurrence by his saying 'anti al-ṭalāq', because the source (maṣdar) is mentioned and the agent noun (ism al-fāʿil) is intended. It is said: a just man (rajul ʿadl) meaning just (ʿādil). So mentioning divorce becomes mentioning the divorcing one, so it becomes like his saying 'anti ṭāliq'. As for occurrence by the other two, it is apparent; because if he mentioned 'anti ṭāliq', divorce occurs, so if he mentions it and mentions the source with it, the source adds emphasis, so it is more appropriate. As for the occurrence being revocable, because it is explicit and does not need intention. (Mustakhlas) One: i.e., one divorce because it is the reality of his speech. (Ayni) Or two: i.e., if he intends in these three forms two divorces, it is also one according to us; because the source (maṣdar) does not bear number. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik said: intention of two is valid, because if intention of three is valid, then intention of two is valid, since two is part of three. We said: the meaning of unity (tawḥīd) is observed in words of singularity (wuḥdān). Observance of the meaning of unity is either by individuality (fardiyyah) or by genericness (jinsiyyah). The dual is far from both, being pure number. Except if the woman is a slave, because two is the generic number of her divorce, so the meaning of unity is observed in the intention of two by consideration of genericness. The conclusion is that the source does not indicate number; it indicates the real individual or the notional individual. And three is a notional individual for a free woman because it is the generic number of her divorce, and likewise two for a slave woman. So it is established that intention of three is permissible by mentioning the source, but not intention of two, because it is pure number, and the generic word does not indicate it, so his intention is futile. (Mustakhlas, Fath) So three: i.e., three occur, because it is the divorce not from the perspective of count. (Ayni) And if he says: 'anti ṭāliq al-ṭalāq', and says: 'I meant by my words: ṭāliq one, and by 'al-ṭalāq' another,' he is believed, because each of them is suitable for effecting divorce by implying 'anti'. So it becomes like his saying: 'anti ṭāliq, anti al-ṭalāq', and two revocable divorces occur if she is consummated; otherwise the second speech is void. (Fath) And if he adds the divorce – the addition here means specification, i.e., if he specifies the divorce to the totality of the woman, like his saying 'anti ṭāliq', the addition is in reality. In what expresses the totality, the addition is metaphorical. And it is also metaphorical to effect divorce with the word of source (maṣdar). Divorce occurs in addition to the totality because the woman is its locus. In what expresses the totality, it is because she is mentioned and the totality is intended, like 'the neck' (al-raqabah), for it intends the self, as in His statement: 'then the freeing of a neck' (4:92). Talaq — Divorce
advanced
10 min
Explicit Divorce Words: Intention for Two vs Three
Ruling on 'ant al-talaq' and effect of intention for two or three

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between intending two and three divorces when using the explicit phrase 'ant al-talaq' and state the practical consequence of this distinction.

Core Idea
The masdar indicates the genus of divorce, not the dual; intention of two is void for a free woman, three is valid as her jins.

When a husband says 'ant al-talaq' (you are divorce), the word 'talaq' is a masdar used as predicate, making it explicit. But what if he intends a specific number of divorces? The Hanafi school has a nuanced position: intention of three is valid for a free woman, but intention of two is not. This lesson unpacks the linguistic and legal reasoning behind this distinction and the objections from other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the explicit phrase and its default ruling
Recognize that 'ant al-talaq' is a sarih (explicit) phrase; it effects one raj'iyyah divorce without any need for intention.
The masdar here is definite (al-talaq) and acts as a predicate, meaning 'you are divorcing'.
2
Understand the Hanafi position on intended numbers
State the ruling: if the husband intends one or two, only one divorce occurs; if he intends three, three occur. For a slave-girl, intention of two is valid because two is her jins.
The key is the concept of jins (genus) and fard i'tibari (virtual individual).
3
Follow the opposing argument
Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik argue: if three is valid (as a part of jins), then two should also be valid since it is part of three.
They see a logical inconsistency in allowing a larger number but not a smaller one.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The masdar does not indicate number; it indicates the jins. The dual is pure number, not a jins. Three is a virtual individual because it is the complete divorce for a free woman, thus it can be intended.
Hanafis use the principle that the jins can be intended as a whole, but its parts (like two) are not indicated.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In practice, if a free husband intends two, only one divorce occurs in Hanafi school; in Shafi'i school, two occur. This affects whether the woman becomes irrevocably divorced after one more divorce.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf: the number of divorces in effect.

(ع) أو ثنتين: أي إذا نوى في هذه الصور الثلاث طلقتين يكون واحدة أيضاً عندنا؛ لأن المصدر لا يحتمل العدد، وقال زفر والشافعي ومالك تصح نية الثنتين، لأنه إذا صح نية الثلاث تصح نية الثنتين؛ لأنهما بعض الثلاث، قلنا إن معنى التوحيد مراعى في الألفاظ الوحدان ورعاية معنى التوحيد إما بالفردية أو الجنسية، والمثنى بمعزل منهما؛ لكونه عدداً محضاً إلا أن تكون المرأة أمة؛ لأن ثنتين جنس طلاقها، فيكون معنى التوحيد مراعياً في نية الثنتين باعتبار الجنسية والحاصل أن المصدر لا يدل على العدد، وإنما يدل على الفرد الحقيقي أو الفرد الاعتباري والثلاث فرد اعتباري في الحرة؛ لكونه جنس طلاقها، وكذا الثنتان في الأمة، فثبت أن نية الثلاث جائزة بذكر المصدر دون نية الثنتين؛ لأنه عدد محض، فلفظ الجنس لا يدل عليه فتلغو نيته. (ع)] ولو قال: أنت طالق الطلاق، وقال: جنس عنيت بقولي: طالق" واحدة وبقولي "الطلاق" أخرى صدق؛ لأن كل واحدة منهما تصلح للإيقاع بإضمار أنت، فصار كقوله : "أنت طالق أنت "الطلاق فيقع رجعيتان إن كانت مدخولاً بها وإلا لغا الكلام الثاني. (فتح)
Chapter of Divorce: Explicit words of divorce: Or al-ibana (separation) or he did not intend anything, and if he said: 'You are divorce' or 'You are divorced the divorce' or 'You are divorced' or he intended separation, the husband to his wife by mentioning the source noun definite for emphasis: 'divorce' falls as one raj'iyyah without intention, or if he intended one or two, and if he intended three then three, the source noun indefinite for emphasis i.e., one divorce meaning by these words one divorce. And if he added divorce to her entirety or to what expresses it like the neck. i.e., or he added the sentence with these words i.e., three divorces without intending anything: because the intended meaning appeared so the ruling attaches to the exact wording. And if he said: 'You are divorced' with sukun on the ta, it does not occur except with intention. (T,A) 'You are divorce' i.e., you are divorced like a just man or you are the one with divorce by deleting the appended. (M) One raj'iyyah: i.e., it occurs by these three forms one raj'iyyah. As for occurrence by his saying 'You are divorce': because the source noun is mentioned and the active participle is meant; it is said: a just man i.e., just, so mentioning divorce is mentioning the divorcer, so it becomes like his saying 'You are divorced'. As for occurrence by the other two, it is apparent; because if he mentioned 'you are divorced', divorce occurs, and if he mentioned it and the source noun with it, the source noun increases emphasis, so it is more appropriate. As for it being raj'iyyah: because it is explicit and does not need intention. (M) 'One' i.e., one divorce because that is the reality of his speech. (A) 'Or two': i.e., if he intended in these three forms two divorces, it is also one according to us; because the source noun does not bear number. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik said: intention of two is valid, because if intention of three is valid, intention of two is valid since it is part of three. We said: the meaning of singularity is considered in singular words; the consideration of singularity is either by individuality or by generality, and the dual is far from both because it is pure number. Except if the woman is a slave-girl: because two is the genus of her divorce, so the meaning of singularity is considered in the intention of two by way of generality. The summary is that the source noun does not indicate number; it only indicates a real individual or a virtual individual, and three is a virtual individual for a free woman because it is the genus of her divorce; similarly two for a slave-girl. So it is established that intention of three is permitted by mentioning the source noun, but not intention of two because it is pure number, and the word for genus does not indicate it, so his intention is void. (M,F) 'So three': [i.e., three occur because it is the divorce not from the perspective of numerality. (A)] And if he said: 'You are divorced the divorce' and said: I meant by my saying 'divorced' one and by my saying 'the divorce' another, he is believed; because each of them is suitable for effecting divorce by assuming 'you are', so it becomes like his saying: 'You are divorced, you are divorce', so it falls as two raj'iyyah if she was consummated, otherwise the second speech is void. (F)
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the effect of intention on explicit divorce words. The Hanafi position is established on the principle that the source noun (masdar) when used as a predicate (like 'ant al-talaq') indicates the verbal noun and is intended to mean the active participle, thus it is as explicit as 'ant taliq'. The occurrence is one raj'iyyah divorce. The dispute arises regarding the validity of intending two or three divorces. Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and Malik argue that if intention of three is valid (as per the Hanafi view that the masdar can indicate a virtual individual and three is a virtual individual for a free woman), then intention of two should also be valid because two is part of three. The Hanafi response distinguishes between the dual and the plural: the masdar indicates singularity by way of jins (genus) or fard (individual). The dual is pure number and not borne by the masdar. However, for a slave-girl, two is her jins (since her divorce is two), so intention of two is valid. For a free woman, intention of three is valid as it is her jins. Thus, the Hanafi ruling is that intention of two is invalid for a free woman. The passage also discusses the scenario where a husband says 'ant taliq al-talaq' and claims he intended separate divorces; he is believed, and if she was consummated, two raj'iyyah divorces occur.

Key Terms
صريح (sarih): Explicit words of divorce that do not require intention to effect divorce.
المصدر (al-masdar): The verbal noun; here it refers to the word 'talāq' used as the predicate.
فرد اعتباري (fard i'tibari): A virtual individual; a number that is considered as a single entity due to being the complete divorce for a woman.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling if a free husband says to his free wife 'ant al-talaq' and intends two divorces?
A) Two divorces occur, because intention of two is a subset of three.
B) One divorce occurs, because the masdar does not indicate the dual number.
C) Three divorces occur, because the masdar indicates the genus which is three.
D) No divorce occurs, because the phrase is ambiguous and intention is required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling that intention of two is invalid for a free woman is based on the linguistic principle that the masdar indicates the jins (three for free women), not the dual number.

Adding Divorce to Body Parts and Fractions: Hanafi Rules and the Shafi'i-Zufar Dispute
Explains rulings for attaching divorce to specific body parts or fractions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between body parts that trigger divorce and those that do not according to the Hanafi school, and explain the legal reasoning for the Hanafi position against Imam al-Shafi'i and Zufar.

Core Idea
Divorce falls only on parts representing the whole or a common share; isolated limbs like a hand do not.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi ruling on attaching divorce to specific body parts or fractions. The core issue is whether saying 'Your hand is divorced' or 'Half of you is divorced' constitutes a valid divorce. The Hanafi school, relying on the purpose of divorce, holds that only parts that signify the whole or an undivided fraction can be loci of divorce.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish body parts that cause divorce from those that do not
From the passage, list the parts that cause divorce (neck, spirit, body, etc.) and those that do not (hand, foot, anus). Note that common fractions like half also cause divorce.
The passage explicitly lists these in the first few lines.
2
Understand the concept of 'common part' (juz' sha'i')
A common part is an undivided fraction that can be the subject of transactions. Because it represents the whole, divorce attached to it is valid and spreads to the whole.
Compare with sale, where a common part can be sold separately.
3
Identify the Hanafi rationale
The Hanafi proof: divorce is to remove the marriage bond; its locus must be something that can receive marriage. A hand cannot receive marriage, so it cannot be a valid locus for divorce. A common part can.
This is a talil (reasoning) based on the purpose of divorce.
4
Analyze the opposing argument (Zufar and al-Shafi'i)
They argue that a hand is enjoyed in marriage, so it should be a valid locus. The Hanafi response distinguishes marriage from divorce: in divorce, the prohibition aspect prevents spreading.
The difference is whether the illa is enjoyment or capacity to receive marriage.
5
Apply the ruling to edge cases
Consider if a community conventionally uses 'hand' to mean the whole person. In that case, divorce would occur. Also, note that hair, nail, etc., are unanimously not grounds for divorce.
The text mentions consensus on hair, nail, etc., except from Malik and al-Shafi'i.

Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: She is divorced by adding to the hand, foot, and anus, because it is a part enjoyed by the marriage contract, so it becomes a locus for divorce, then the ruling is established by the addition, then it spreads to the whole as in the common part, unlike adding marriage to these three parts, because prohibition does not overcome permissibility, but in divorce the opposite applies, so the prohibited overcomes the permissible. Our proof: Divorce is legislated to remove the bond, so it is specific to the locus of the bond, and its locus is what it is permissible to add marriage to, and it is not permissible to add marriage to the hand, unlike the common part, which is suitable for addition, so it is a locus for divorce. They differed about the back and belly; the more apparent is that it does not occur because they do not express the entire body. If he adds to hair, nail, tooth, saliva, vein, it does not occur by consensus except in a narration from Malik and al-Shafi'i; about blood there are two narrations. If it is said: The hand and heart are used to express the whole, as in Allah's words "May the hands of Abu Lahab perish" (Masad:1) and his saying "The hand is responsible for what it takes" and Allah's words "His heart is sinful" (Baqara:283), we say: This usage did not continue in language or custom; it only came rarely, such that if among a people they express the whole by it, then divorce occurs by any part. (Mustakhlas, Fath, 'Ayni) And half the divorce: by saying 'You are divorced half a divorce.' (T) Or a third of it: i.e., a third of a divorce by saying 'You are divorced a third of a divorce.' (T) Two divorces: by saying 'You are divorced three halves of two divorces.' (A)
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with its commentary, deals with the ruling when a husband attaches divorce to a part of his wife's body or to a fractional part of her being. The base text states that attaching divorce to the neck, spirit, body, private parts, head, face, or to a common part like half or third results in divorce, while attaching to the hand, foot, or anus does not. The commentator expands: The neck, spirit, etc., are parts that conventionally express the whole person, so divorce falls on the whole. A common part (juz' sha'i') like half or third is the locus of transactions, so it can receive divorce, but since divorce cannot be partial, it spreads to the whole. In contrast, the hand, foot, and anus are specific limbs that do not represent the whole; thus divorce does not fall. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that these limbs are enjoyed in marriage, so they should be valid loci for divorce, just like a common part. The Hanafi response is that divorce is designed to remove the marriage bond, so its locus must be something to which marriage can be attached; marriage cannot be attached to a hand, but can be attached to a common part. Therefore, the Hanafi position stands. The passage also notes that if a particular community conventionally uses a limb (like hand) to denote the whole, then divorce would occur. The case of hair, nail, etc., is unanimously not divorce except in a weak narration. For the back and belly, the preferred view is no divorce. The fractional part of a divorce (e.g., half a divorce) is also discussed: saying 'half a divorce' results in one complete divorce because a fraction of a divorce cannot be recognized; it becomes a whole. Similarly, saying 'three halves of two divorces' results in three divorces.

Key Terms
جزء شائع (juz' sha'i'): A common or undifferentiated part of a thing, like half or third, that can be the subject of transactions.
طلاق (talaq): The dissolution of a marriage contract using the word talaq or its derivatives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a husband says to his wife 'Your hand is divorced'?
A) One divorce occurs immediately.
B) No divorce occurs.
C) Two divorces occur.
D) It depends on intention.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a divorce attached to a specific limb (e.g., hand) does not take effect; only parts that represent the whole or a common fraction do.

The Ruling on Divorce with Numerical Ranges: From One to Three
Intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on interpreting 'from X to Y' in divorce pronouncements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on divorce with numerical ranges and explain how the inclusion or exclusion of limits affects the count.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'from one to three', Abu Hanifa counts two; Sahibayn count three; Zufar counts one — based on whether limits are included.

When a husband says 'You are divorced from one to three', the number of divorces that occur depends on how we interpret the phrase. Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar have different rules on whether the starting and ending numbers are included. This lesson examines their positions and the role of customary usage (urf) in resolving the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
For the phrase 'from one to two' and 'from one to three', state the number of divorces according to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn), and Zufar.
Abu Hanifa: one from one to two, two from one to three; Sahibayn: two from one to two, three from one to three; Zufar: none from one to two, one from one to three.
2
Understand the principle on ghaya (limit)
Abu Hanifa includes the starting limit (ibtida' al-ghaya) but excludes the ending limit (intiha' al-ghaya). Sahibayn include both. Zufar excludes both.
The principle is applied to the numbers: the smaller number is the starting limit, the larger is the ending limit.
3
Analyze the proof for Abu Hanifa's view
The passage presents an anecdote where al-Asma'i asks Zufar about age statements: if someone says 'between sixty and seventy', it does not mean sixty or seventy; similarly, in divorce, 'between one and three' excludes the ends. This illustrates istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom.
Custom (urf) shows that when specifying a range, the speaker intends a number between the limits, not the limits themselves.
4
Determine the practical consequence and mufta bihi
The difference affects the number of divorces that take effect. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is Abu Hanifa's view, due to the strength of customary usage and the istihsan argument.
Tamarah al-khilaf: the same phrase yields 1, 2, or 3 divorces depending on the position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط،ع)] أي ثلاث تطليقات؛ لأن نصف التطليقتين ،تطليقة، فإن جمع بين ثلاثة أنصاف تطليقتين يقع ثلاث تطليقات ضرورة، ولو قال: أنت طالق نصف تطليقة وثلث تطليقة وسدس تطليقة وهي مدخول بها طلقت ثلاثاً؛ لأنه أوقع من كل تطليقة جزءاً فيتكامل كل جزء؛ لأنه ذكر كل تطليقة منكراً، والمنكر إذا أعيد منكرا يكون غير الأول، بخلاف ما إذا قال: أنت طالق نصف تطليقة وثلثها وسدسها حيث تطلق واحدة؛ لأن الثاني والثالث معرف، فيكون عين الأول، فتكون الأجزاء من طلقة واحدة فيضم بعضها إلى بعض حتى تكمل رمز الحقائق: ۲۳۷/۱] (فتح) ومن واحدة أي لو قال: أنت طالق من واحدة إلى ثنتين. (ط) أو ما بين أي لو قال: أنت طالق ما بين إلخ. (ط) واحدة: [مرفوع على أنه فاعل لفعل محذوف أي تقع واحدة، أو خبر مبتدأ محذوف أي فالواقع واحدة. (ع)] أي لو قال: أنت طالق من واحدة إلى ثنتين أو ما بين واحدة إلى ثنتين فالواقع تطليقة واحدة، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الأصل عند أبي حنيفة أن ابتداء الغاية يدخل لا انتهاءها، وعندهما تقع ثنتان؛ لأن الغايتين تدخلان جميعاً زفر : الغايتان لا تدخلان فلا يقع في هذه الصورة شيء عنده، وقوله: "إلى ثلاث ثنتان"، هذا أيضاً عند أبي حنيفة؛ لأن الانتهاء وهو قوله ثلاث ليس بداخل وعندهما: تقع ثلاث تطليقات؛ لدخول الابتداء والانتهاء، وعند زفر واحدة؛ لعدم دخول الابتداء والانتهاء، وقد حاج الأصمعي زفر في هذه المسألة على باب الرشيد، فقال: ما تقول في رجل قال لامرأته: أنت طالق ما بين واحدة إلى ثلاث قال: تطلق واحدة؛ لأن كلمة "ما بين" لا تتناول الحدين. عندهما، من وعند قال الأصمعي له ما تقول في رجل قيل له : كم سنك؟ فقال: ما بين ستين إلى سبعين، أيكون ابن تسع سنين؟ فتحير، فقال: استحسن يعني أقول بالاستحسان لا بالقياس في مثل هذا لأبي حنيفة أن مثل هذا الكلام يراد به الأكثر من الأقل، والأقل من الأكثر عرفاً يقال: سن فلان بين ستين إلى سبعين يراد به الأكثر من الستين والأقل السبعين، والخلاف بين الإمام والصاحبين فيما أصله الحظر وفيما مرجعه الإباحة كخذ من مالي من مائة إلى ألف دخول الغايتين اتفاقاً، ولو قال: من واحدة إلى واحدة قيل: على الخلاف فلا شيء عند زفر، وعندهما ثنتان، وعند أبي حنيفة واحدة، وقيل: يقع واحدة بالاتفاق؛ لاستحالة أن يكون الشيء الواحد حداً ومحدودا فيلغو، ويبقى قوله: أنت طالق ويقع به واحدة كما مر ولو قال: "ما بين واحدة وثلاث" يقع واحدة، يروى ذلك أبي يوسف بخلاف ما إذا كان غاية. (عيني، فتح) عن " ثنتان أي لو قال: أنت طالق من واحدة إلى ثلاث فالواقع ثنتان وكذا لو قال ما بين واحدة إلى ثلاث. (ط،ع)
Adding divorce to the number: Three. And 'from one' or 'what is between one to two' is one. And 'to three' is two. 'Three' in nominative as predicate of the subject, meaning three divorces. And if he said 'three halves of a divorce' the correct is that two fall. (T, A) i.e., three divorces because half of two divorces is one divorce, so combining three halves of two divorces necessitates three divorces. And if he said 'You are divorced half a divorce and a third of a divorce and a sixth of a divorce' and she is one with whom he has consummated, she is divorced three times because he has caused a part of each divorce to occur, so each part becomes complete because he mentioned each divorce as indefinite, and when an indefinite is repeated as indefinite, it is different from the first, unlike if he said 'You are divorced half a divorce and its third and its sixth' where she gets one divorce because the second and third are definite and thus the same as the first, so the parts are from one divorce and they are combined until completion. [Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/237] (Fath) And 'from one' i.e., if he said 'You are divorced from one to two' (T) or 'what is between' i.e., if he said 'You are divorced what is between' etc. (T) 'One' is nominative as subject of an omitted verb meaning one falls, or predicate of omitted subject meaning the operative is one. (A) i.e., if he said 'You are divorced from one to two' or 'what is between one to two', the operative is one divorce. This is according to Abu Hanifa because his principle is that the beginning of the limit is included but not the end. According to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) two fall because both limits are included. Zufar: both limits are not included, so nothing falls in this case. And his saying 'to three is two' is also according to Abu Hanifa because the end which is 'three' is not included, while according to them three divorces fall due to inclusion of beginning and end, and according to Zufar one due to non-inclusion of beginning and end. Al-Asma'i debated Zufar in this matter at the court of al-Rashid. He said: What do you say about a man who said to his wife 'You are divorced what is between one to three'? He said: She gets one divorce because the phrase 'what is between' does not include the two limits. Al-Asma'i said: What do you say about a man who was asked 'How old are you?' and he said 'Between sixty and seventy', would he be nine years old? Zufar was perplexed and then said: I say by istihsan, not by qiyas, in such matters. For Abu Hanifa, such speech customarily intends the greater from the less and the less from the greater. For example, one says 'So-and-so's age is between sixty and seventy' meaning the greater from sixty and the less from seventy. The difference between the Imam and the two companions is in matters that are originally prohibitory or permissive, like 'Take from my wealth from one hundred to one thousand' where inclusion of both limits is agreed. And if he said 'from one to one', it is said there is disagreement: nothing from Zufar, two from them, one from Abu Hanifa. And it is said one falls by consensus because it is impossible for a single thing to be both limit and limited, so it becomes null, leaving his statement 'You are divorced', which causes one divorce as before. And if he said 'what is between one and three' one divorce falls, narrated from Abu Yusuf, contrary to when there is a limit. ('Ayni, Fath) 'Two' i.e., if he said 'You are divorced from one to three' then the operative is two, and similarly if he said 'what is between one to three'. (T, A)

Commentary
This passage continues the exposition on adding divorce to numbers. The matn states: 'Three' means three divorces; 'from one or between one to two' is one; 'to three' is two. The commentator explains the details. If a husband says 'You are divorced half a divorce and a third and a sixth', and she is a consummated wife, she gets three divorces because each fraction is from a separate divorce (indefinite repetition indicates different divorces). If he says 'half a divorce and its third and its sixth', she gets one divorce because the fractions refer back to the same divorce (definite). Then the main issue: if he says 'from one to two', Abu Hanifa rules one divorce because only the starting limit (one) is included; the Sahibayn say two because both limits are included; Zufar says none because neither is included. For 'from one to three', Abu Hanifa says two (one and two are included? Actually one and two? Wait: 'to three' is two at Abu Hanifa: meaning one and two? The text says 'to three is two' meaning two divorces fall (one and two) because the end (three) is excluded. Sahibayn say three; Zufar says one. The anecdote shows Zufar's inconsistency is resolved by istihsan (customary usage). The custom in Arabic is that 'between X and Y' means more than X and less than Y, so when saying 'between sixty and seventy', it means an age between them, not 60 or 70. Applied to divorce, 'between one and three' means a number between them, which is two, but the Hanafi position here is different? Actually for 'from one to three' Abu Hanifa says two (i.e., one and two? Wait: 'to three' in the first part: 'and to three is two' - from context: if he says 'from one to three', the operative is two (i.e., one and two) because the end three is not included. So the phrase 'between one and three' yields one according to Abu Yusuf (narrated), because 'between' excludes both ends. The passage clarifies that the ikhtilaf arises only when the matter is originally prohibitory? Actually it says the ikhtilaf is in matters where the original is prohibition or permissibility, but for pure permission like 'take from my wealth from 100 to 1000', both limits are included by agreement.

Key Terms
ابتداء الغاية (ibtida' al-ghaya): The starting point of a limit; in divorce phrases like 'from one', the number 'one' is the beginning.
انتهاء الغاية (intiha' al-ghaya): The endpoint of a limit; e.g., 'to two' or 'to three'.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference, a method of departing from strict analogy (qiyas) in favor of a more equitable outcome based on custom or public interest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Two divorces.
C) Three divorces.
D) None.

Key Takeaway
When pronouncing divorce with numerical ranges, Hanafi muftis follow Abu Hanifa's principle: include the starting number, exclude the ending number.

Adding Divorce to Number: Limits and Fractions
Rulings and juristic disagreements on divorce tied to numerical expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rules for determining the number of divorces when a numerical range is used, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar on the inclusion of limits, and apply the rules for fractional divorces and definite/indefinite repetition.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'from one to three', only two divorces occur — the start is included, the end is not, per Abu Hanifa.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses the intricate rulings when divorce pronouncements include numerical expressions. The central dispute among Hanafi imams is whether the beginning and ending limits of a range are both included. Abu Hanifa's view, relying on custom and istihsan, is the relied-upon position. The passage also covers fractional divorces and the effect of definite vs. indefinite repetition of parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling for numerical ranges
The matn states: 'from one to two' yields one divorce (Abu Hanifa); 'from one to three' yields two. Note the disagreement with the Sahibayn and Zufar.
2
Understand the dispute on limit inclusion
Abu Hanifa includes only the starting limit; the Sahibayn include both; Zufar excludes both. This leads to different counts: for 'from one to three', Abu Hanifa = 2, Sahibayn = 3, Zufar = 1.
The principle: al-ghayah (limit) inclusion varies.
3
Apply istihsan: the story of Al-Asma'i
Al-Asma'i challenged Zufar with an age analogy: if a man says his age is between sixty and seventy, he is not nine. This shows custom overrides strict qiyas, supporting Abu Hanifa's view.
Istihsan corrects extreme analogy.
4
Analyze fractional divorces and repeated parts
When fractions like half, third, sixth are mentioned, if each part is indefinite (nakirah), they form separate divorces. If repeated with the definite article (ma'rifah), they combine into one.
Indefinite repetition = multiple divorces; definite repetition = single divorce.
5
Distinguish definite vs. indefinite repetition
For example, 'half a divorce, a third of a divorce, a sixth of a divorce' (all indefinite) yields three divorces. 'Half a divorce, its third, its sixth' (definite) yields one divorce.
The rule: al-nakirah idha u'idat nakirah takun ghayr al-ula.

And it is said: one occurs by agreement; because it is impossible for one thing to be a boundary and the bounded, so it is meaningless, and his statement 'You are divorced' remains, and one occurs by it as previously. And if he said: 'between one and three', one occurs, narrated from Abu Yusuf, unlike if it were a limit.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq discusses the rulings when a husband attaches a number to the divorce pronouncement. The matn states several basic rules: if he says 'three' (i.e., 'you are divorced three'), three divorces occur. If he says 'from one to two' or 'between one to two', only one divorce occurs according to Abu Hanifa; but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) say two, and Zufar says nothing. Similarly, 'from one to three' yields two according to Abu Hanifa, three according to the Sahibayn, and one according to Zufar. The commentary explains the reasoning: Abu Hanifa considers that the starting limit is included but the ending limit is not, while the Sahibayn include both, and Zufar excludes both. The famous story of Al-Asma'i challenging Zufar illustrates the point: if a man says his age is between sixty and seventy, no one thinks he is nine; custom understands the intended range. Thus istihsan overrides strict analogy. The passage also covers the case where half-divorces are combined and where fractions are specified with indefinite vs. definite articles. The Hanafi position is that repeated indefinite parts form separate divorces, while definite parts combine into one. The source of authority for the basic rulings is Zahir al-Riwayah, and the later relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that inclusion of limits depends on context: in prohibitive matters, both limits are included by agreement; in permissive matters, the ikhtilaf remains. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) is that a husband's intent and custom can affect the number of divorces.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghayah): The end point or limit in a range (e.g., 'three' in 'from one to three').
الظرف (al-zarf): A container; used metaphorically for the number that is supposed to contain the divorce (e.g., 'in two' as a container for 'one').
المنكر (al-nakirah): Indefinite noun; when repeated as indefinite, it is considered a separate entity.
المعرف (al-ma'rifah): Definite noun; when repeated with the definite article, it refers to the same entity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a divorce is tied to a numerical range, only the starting limit is included, so the number of divorces is one less than the upper bound given.

Divorce Tied to Place: Spatial Expressions and the Revocable–Irrevocable Dispute
Rules for spatial expressions in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi position that spatial qualifications in divorce do not make it irrevocable (ba'in) and distinguish it from Zufar's view by analyzing the legal reasoning (dalil) for both sides.

Core Idea
Spatial expressions in divorce describe brevity, not length, so the divorce remains revocable.

When a husband says 'You are divorced from here to Syria,' or 'in Mecca,' the question arises: does the location affect the type of divorce? The Hanafi school holds that such phrases do not change the revocable nature, while Zufar argues that kinayah (allusion) implies length and thus makes it irrevocable. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on spatial expressions
Identify that Hanafis rule the divorce as one revocable (raj'i) for 'from here to Syria' and as immediately effective everywhere for 'in Mecca'. Write down the exact ruling.
2
Identify Zufar's opposing view and his proof
Zufar says 'from here to Syria' yields one irrevocable (ba'in) divorce. He argues that the phrase is a kinayah implying length, and kinayah in divorce makes it ba'in.
Also note that Zufar's view is shared by Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zufar
Hanafis counter: if the husband explicitly said 'a long divorce,' it would still be revocable, so kinayah should not make it ba'in. Instead, the phrase describes the divorce as short (qasr) because divorce has no length—so it is simply revocable.
The Hanafi response also considers the possibility that 'to Syria' refers to the woman, not the divorce.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Hanafi ruling, the husband can take back his wife during iddah without a new marriage; under Zufar's ruling, reconciliation requires a new marriage and her consent.
5
Apply the rule to other spatial expressions (e.g., 'in Mecca', 'in the house')
Note that for 'in Mecca', divorce occurs immediately everywhere—no spatial restriction. This is consistent with the principle that divorce is not localizable.
The same applies to 'in the sun', 'in a garment', etc.

وواحدة في ثنتين واحدة إن لم ينو أو نوى الضرب، وإن نوى واحدة وثنتين فثلاث، يقع طلقة واحدة رجعية طع والحساب أو لم ينو شيئاً بقوله: واحدة في ثنتين : أي مع ثنتين ز وثنتين في ثنتين ثنتان وإن نوى الضرب، ومن هنا إلى الشام واحدة رجعيّة، وبمكة كانت واحدة إن لم ينو: أي لو قال: أنت طالق واحدة في ثنتين تقع واحدة إن لم ينو الثنتين؛ لأن "في ثنتين" ظرف في حقيقة وهو لا يصلح له فيقع المظروف أي الواحدة لا ما جعله ظرفا أي ثنتين وأما إذا نوى الضرب والحساب فواحدة أيضاً؛ لأن عمل الضرب أثره في تكثير الأجزاء بعدد المضروب فيه لا في زيادة المضروب إذ لو أفادها ما وجد في الدنيا فقير، وتكثير الأجزاء على الطلقة الواحدة لا يوجب تعددها ما لم تزد الأجزاء على الواحدة، زفر ومالك وأحمد والشافعي في قول: يقع ثنتان؛ لعرف الحساب وهو قول الحسن بن زياد، قال سري الدين: أنه رجح في فتح القدير قول زفر وكذا رجحه في غاية البيان تبيين الحقائق: ٥٠/٣] (فتح فثلاث [ أي طلقات يقعن إن كانت مدخولا بها؛ لوجود معنى الجمع وإلا فواحدة. لأنه محتمل كلامه؛ لأن حرف الواو للجمع والظرف يجمع المظروف فيصير في كل منهما أي واحدة وثنتين، وواحدة في ثنتين معنى الجمعية فتطلق ثلاثاً إن كانت مدخولا بها، وواحدة إن كانت غير مدخول بها؛ لأن حرف الواو وإن للجمع لكنه يفيد الترتيب أيضاً إلا أن في غير المدخول بها لما وقعت واحدة لم تبق محلا لوقوع الثنتين فيلغو قوله: "وثنتين"، ولو نوى واحدة مع ثنتين يقع الثلاث في المدخول وغيرها. ثنتان: أي لو قال: أنت طالق ثنتين في ثنتين فالواقع ثنتان؛ لأنه إن نوى الظرف فهو لا يصلح له فيقع المظروف لا ما جعله ظرفا، ولو نوى الضرب فلأن الضرب أثره في تكثير الأجزاء، وكثرة الأجزاء في الطلاق غير معتبرة على ما عرف فيقع ثنتان، وقال زفر : تطلق ثلاثاً اعتباراً بالضرب وبالضرب يكون أربعا لكن لا يزيد الطلاق على الثلاث فلهذا يقع ثلاث. وإن نوى: متصلة أي سواء نوى الحساب أو لم ينو شيئاً. واحدة رجعيّة: أي لو قال: أنت طالق من ههنا إلى الشام فهي واحدة رجعية عندنا، وقال زفر هي واحدة بائنة؛ لأنه وصف الطلاق بالطول، ولا يرد عليه أنه إذا قال لها : أنت طالق تطليقة طويلة يقع الرجعي عنده فكيف يقع البائن هنا؛ لأنه يقول: هنا يثبت الطول بالكناية وبها يقع بائنا بخلاف الصريح، ولنا أنه وصف الطلاق بالقصر ؛ لأن الطلاق متى وقع، وقع في الأماكن ۲۳۸/١](مستخلص) وبمكة إلخ: أي إذا قال لها : أنت طالق بمكة، أو قال: في الدار يقع الطلاق في الحال في كل البلاد والأماكن؛ لأن الطلاق لا يختص بمكان دون مكان، وكذا لو قال: أنت طالق في ثوب كذا أو في الشمس أو في الظل، أو عريضة أو مصلية فيقع في الحال؛ لأن اختصاص الطلاق بهذه الصور وصف حكمي فيعتبر بالحقيقي، والحقيقي .
And 'one in two' is one if he did not intend or intended multiplication, and if he intended one and two then three. One revocable divorce occurs, whether for arithmetic or if he intended nothing. By his saying 'one in two' i.e. with two. And 'two in two' is two even if he intended multiplication. And 'from here to Syria' is one revocable divorce. And 'in Mecca' it was one if he did not intend. i.e. if he said: You are divorced one in two, one occurs if he did not intend the two; because 'in two' is a container in reality and it is not suitable for it, so the contained falls, which is the one, not what he made a container i.e. two. And as for if he intended multiplication and arithmetic, it is also one; because the effect of multiplication is to multiply parts by the number of the multiplier, not to increase the multiplicand, for if it benefited that, no poor person would exist in the world. And multiplying parts on one divorce does not necessitate its multiplicity as long as the parts do not exceed one. Zufar, Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion: two occur, due to the custom of arithmetic. This is the saying of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. Saray al-Din said: he preferred in Fath al-Qadir the saying of Zufar, and likewise preferred it in Ghayat al-Bayan. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq: 50/3. (Fath) So three: i.e. divorces occur if she is one with whom he has consummated; due to existence of the meaning of combination, otherwise one. Because his speech is probable; because the letter waw is for combination and the container combines the contained, so it becomes in each of them, i.e. one and two. And 'one in two' has the meaning of combination, so she is divorced three if she is consummated, and one if she is not consummated; because the waw, although for combination, also indicates order, except that in the non-consummated case, when one has fallen, she is no longer a locus for the falling of the two, so his saying 'and two' becomes meaningless. And if he intended one with two, three occur in the consummated and others. Two: i.e. if he said: You are divorced two in two, what occurs is two; because if he intended the container, it is not suitable for it, so the contained falls, not what he made a container. And if he intended multiplication, then multiplication's effect is to multiply parts, and multiplicity of parts in divorce is not considered as known, so two occur. And Zufar said: she is divorced three, considering multiplication, and by multiplication it would be four, but divorce does not exceed three, so three occurs. And 'if he intended' is connected, i.e. whether he intended arithmetic or nothing. One revocable: i.e. if he said: You are divorced from here to Syria, it is one revocable according to us. And Zufar said: it is one irrevocable (ba'in); because he described divorce with length. And it is not objected that if he said to her: You are divorced a long divorce, the revocable occurs according to him, so how does the irrevocable occur here? Because he says: here length is established by kinayah (allusion), and by it the irrevocable occurs, unlike the explicit. And for us: he described divorce with shortness; because whenever divorce occurs, it occurs in all places, and divorce itself does not admit shortness; because it is not a body, so its ruling is shortened by being revocable. And it is said: his saying 'to Syria' refers to the woman, not the divorce, so that if he said 'a divorce to Syria' with the additional word 'divorce', it would be irrevocable. Rampur al-Haqa'iq: 238/1 (Mustakhlas) And 'in Mecca' etc.: i.e. if he said to her: You are divorced in Mecca, or he said: in the house, divorce occurs immediately in all lands and places; because divorce is not specific to one place over another. Likewise if he said: You are divorced in such-and-such a garment, or in the sun, or in the shade, or in a prayer rug, it occurs immediately; because the specification of divorce to these images is a hukmi description, so it is considered by the real, and the real...
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq covers rulings when divorce is linked to a spatial expression. The main points: (1) 'One in two' – if the husband says 'you are divorced one in two', the basic ruling is that one revocable divorce occurs, whether he intends the arithmetic meaning (multiplication) or not. If he intends 'one and two' (using 'in' as 'and'), then three divorces occur for a consummated wife, one for an unconsummated wife. (2) 'Two in two' yields two divorces. Zufar holds that it yields three due to multiplication. (3) 'From here to Syria' – Hanafis say one revocable divorce; Zufar says one irrevocable (ba'in). The Hanafi reasoning: the phrase describes the divorce as having a short duration, so it remains revocable. (4) 'In Mecca' or 'in the house' – divorce occurs immediately everywhere, because divorce cannot be localized. The passage also discusses the difference between explicit and kinayah expressions, and the effect of custom. The authority is from the Mustakhlas and other later sources, but the rulings align with Zahir al-Riwayah. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position that such spatial qualifications do not affect the nature (ba'in or raj'i) of the divorce, except as specified.

Key Terms
الرجعية (al-raj'iyyah): Revocable divorce, where the husband can take back his wife during the waiting period without a new marriage.
البائن (al-ba'in): Irrevocable divorce, where the husband cannot take back his wife except with a new marriage and her consent.
الكناية (al-kinayah): An indirect or allusive expression that implies divorce but is not explicit. In Hanafi law, kinayah divorce is effective if the husband intends divorce, and may be revocable or irrevocable depending on context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One revocable divorce
B) One irrevocable divorce
C) Three revocable divorces
D) No divorce

Key Takeaway
Spatial expressions in divorce are interpreted as revocable because divorce is formless and occurs everywhere immediately.

One Divorce or Two? The Hanafi Position on Saying 'One in Two'
Ruling on saying 'you are divorced one in two' with intent and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that one revocable divorce falls when a husband says 'one in two' intending arithmetic, and evaluate the opposing view of Zufar.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, 'one in two' defaults to one divorce because multiplication affects parts not the count; intent of both numbers is needed for three.

Divorce formulas can be ambiguous. When a husband says 'you are divorced one in two,' the number of divorces depends on his intent and the interpretation of the phrase. This passage from Kanz al-Daqa’iq examines the Hanafi ruling, highlighting a dispute within the school and with other scholars. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to analyze legal language and intention.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and possible meanings
The phrase 'one in two' (واحدة في ثنتين) can be read as 'one with two' (addition) or 'one into two' (multiplication). The ruling turns on which meaning the husband intended.
The Arabic 'fī' is ambiguous; it can mean 'with' or 'in' (multiplication).
2
State the default Hanafi position
According to Abu Hanifa, if the husband did not intend anything specific, or intended the arithmetic operation (multiplication), one revocable divorce falls. The container 'in two' does not increase the count.
The default is one, as the contained (one) falls, not the container (two).
3
Analyze the case of intending 'one and two'
If the husband intended both numbers (one and two) together, then three divorces fall if the marriage was consummated; otherwise one. This is because the conjunction (waw) joins the numbers, making a total of three.
For a non-consummated wife, one divorce ends the marriage, so the additional 'two' is ineffective.
4
Examine the opposing view (Zufar et al.)
Zufar, Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi‘i hold that when the husband intends arithmetic, two divorces fall, based on the customary understanding that 'one in two' means multiplication yielding two.
This view relies on the common usage in arithmetic, not just linguistic analysis.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi response and tarjih
The Hanafi response emphasizes that multiplication increases parts (e.g., units of a number) but not the number itself. One divorce remains one. Later scholars like Sari al-Din preferred Zufar’s view, but the authoritative (mufta bihi) position remains Abu Hanifa’s.
The dispute shows how legal reasoning can differ on the same phrase.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وواحدة في ثنتين واحدة إن لم ينو أو نوى الضرب، وإن نوى واحدة وثنتين فثلاث، يقع طلقة واحدة رجعية طع والحساب أو لم ينو شيئاً بقوله: واحدة في ثنتين : أي مع ثنتين ز واحدة إن لم ينو: أي لو قال: أنت طالق واحدة في ثنتين تقع واحدة إن لم ينو الثنتين؛ لأن "في ثنتين" ظرف في حقيقة وهو لا يصلح له فيقع المظروف أي الواحدة لا ما جعله ظرفا أي ثنتين وأما إذا نوى الضرب والحساب فواحدة أيضاً؛ لأن عمل الضرب أثره في تكثير الأجزاء بعدد المضروب فيه لا في زيادة المضروب إذ لو أفادها ما وجد في الدنيا فقير، وتكثير الأجزاء على الطلقة الواحدة لا يوجب تعددها ما لم تزد الأجزاء على الواحدة، زفر ومالك وأحمد والشافعي في قول: يقع ثنتان؛ لعرف الحساب وهو قول الحسن بن زياد، قال سري الدين: أنه رجح في فتح القدير قول زفر وكذا رجحه في غاية البيان تبيين الحقائق: ٥٠/٣] (فتح فثلاث [ أي طلقات يقعن إن كانت مدخولا بها؛ لوجود معنى الجمع وإلا فواحدة. (ط،ع)] لأنه محتمل كلامه؛ لأن حرف الواو للجمع والظرف يجمع المظروف فيصير في كل منهما أي واحدة وثنتين، وواحدة في ثنتين معنى الجمعية فتطلق ثلاثاً إن كانت مدخولا بها، وواحدة إن كانت غير مدخول بها؛ لأن حرف الواو وإن للجمع لكنه يفيد الترتيب أيضاً إلا أن في غير المدخول بها لما وقعت واحدة لم تبق محلا لوقوع الثنتين فيلغو قوله: "وثنتين"، ولو نوى واحدة مع ثنتين يقع الثلاث في المدخول وغيرها. (مستخلص)
And 'one in two': one divorce falls if he did not intend or intended the arithmetic, and if he intended one and two, then three; one revocable divorce falls, whether [intending] arithmetic or nothing. He said: 'one in two' means with two. 'One if he did not intend': that is, if he said: 'You are divorced one in two', one falls if he did not intend the two; because 'in two' is a place expression in reality and it is not suitable for it, so the contained, i.e. the one, falls, not what he made the container, i.e. two. And if he intended the arithmetic, one also; because the operation of multiplication affects the multiplication of parts by the multiplier, not the increase of the multiplicand, for if it did, no poor person would be found in the world. Multiplying the parts of one divorce does not necessitate its multiplicity unless the parts exceed one. Zufar, Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i in one opinion: two fall, due to the custom of arithmetic. This is the opinion of al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. Sari al-Din said that he gave preponderance to Zufar's view in Fath al-Qadir, and likewise in Ghayat al-Bayan. [One commentary says:] 'then three divorces fall if she is one with whom he has consummated marriage, due to the meaning of conjunction, otherwise one' because his words are probable; for the letter waw is for conjunction and the container collects the contained, so each becomes both, i.e. one and two. And 'one in two' has the meaning of conjunction, so she is divorced three if consummated, and one if not consummated; because the letter waw, although for conjunction, also indicates sequence, except that in the case of the non-consummated wife, when one divorce fell, she is no longer a place for the two to fall, so his words 'and two' are void. And if he intended one with two, three fall for the consummated and other.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute with cross-madhhab elements. The ruling is about a husband saying 'you are divorced one in two' (واحدة في ثنتين). According to Abu Hanifa and the Hanafi school, one revocable divorce falls if the husband did not intend anything or intended the arithmetic operation (multiplication). Even if he intended 'one and two' together, three divorces fall only if the wife was consummated (مدخول بها); otherwise one. The reasoning is that 'in two' is a container expression, and the contained (one) falls, not the container (two). For arithmetic intention, multiplication only increases parts, not the number of divorces, unless parts exceed one. Zufar, Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i hold that two divorces fall due to the custom of arithmetic. The Hanafi position is that the husband's intent is considered, and the default is one unless clearly intending both numbers.

Key Terms
واحدة في ثنتين (wāḥidatan fī ithnatayn): A phrase potentially ambiguous: 'one in two' meaning either 'one with two' (addition) or 'one into two' (multiplication).
الضرب (al-ḍarb): The arithmetic operation of multiplication, here intended by the husband.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the default for the phrase 'one in two' in divorce is one revocable divorce; intention of both numbers is required for three, and arithmetic intent does not multiply the count.

The Hanafi Ruling on 'Two in Two' Divorce and the Dispute with Zufar
Ruling on saying 'two in two' and the dispute with Zufar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'two in two' in divorce and explain why Zufar's opinion of three divorces is not accepted.

Core Idea
Saying 'two in two' in divorce yields two divorces, not four or three, because arithmetic multiplication does not apply to divorce counts.

This passage addresses an ambiguous divorce pronouncement: 'You are divorced two in two.' Is it two divorces, three, or four? The Hanafi school rules that only two divorces occur, regardless of whether the husband intended arithmetic. Zufar, a Hanafi scholar, held that multiplication gives four but is capped at three. Let's examine the reasoning and the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: two divorces fall when the husband says 'two in two'. This is the majority position of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).
The phrase is treated as a repetition, not multiplication.
2
Understand the container argument
Analyse why the container interpretation fails. If the husband intended 'in' as a container, it is invalid because the phrase 'in two' cannot be a container for the divorce itself; only the numbers matter. Hence, two divorces fall.
The contained (two) falls regardless of the container's invalidity.
3
Understand the arithmetic argument
Even if the husband intended multiplication (2×2), the Hanafi rule is that multiplicity of parts in a single expression does not increase the count of divorces. Multiplication only increases parts, but divorce is not divisible that way.
Thus, the same two divorces fall.
4
Identify Zufar's objection
Zufar argued that 'two in two' is arithmetic giving four, but since divorce cannot exceed three, three divorces fall. Note that Zufar is a Hanafi scholar but his view is considered weak within the school.
Zufar's reasoning treats the phrase as a literal mathematical operation.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preference)
The Hanafi school relies on the majority view of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn. The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is that two divorces occur. The passage does not adopt Zufar's view.
This is consistent with the principle that ambiguous expressions in divorce are interpreted to the minimum possible number.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثنتين في ثنتين ثنتان وإن نوى الضرب، ومن هنا إلى الشام واحدة رجعيّة، وبمكة كان أي فالواقع تطليقتان أي يقع طلقة واحدة الله المعين) وعند واحدة إن لم ينو: ... ثنتان: أي لو قال: أنت طالق ثنتين في ثنتين فالواقع ثنتان؛ لأنه إن نوى الظرف فهو لا يصلح له فيقع المظروف لا ما جعله ظرفا، ولو نوى الضرب فلأن الضرب أثره في تكثير الأجزاء، وكثرة الأجزاء في الطلاق غير معتبرة على ما عرف فيقع ثنتان، وقال زفر : تطلق ثلاثاً اعتباراً بالضرب وبالضرب يكون أربعا لكن لا يزيد الطلاق على الثلاث فلهذا يقع ثلاث. (مستخلص، فتح) وإن نوى: متصلة أي سواء نوى الحساب أو لم ينو شيئاً. (ط، ع)
And 'two in two': two divorces [fall], even if he intended the arithmetic. And from here to Syria, one revocable [divorce]. And in Mecca, it was... so the actual is two divorces, i.e. one divorce falls. 'Allah the Helper' and according to 'one if he did not intend'... 'Two in two': that is, if he said: 'You are divorced two in two', the actual is two; because if he intended the container expression, it is not suitable, so the contained falls, not what he made the container; and if he intended the arithmetic, because the effect of arithmetic is to multiply parts, and multiplicity of parts in divorce is not considered as known, so two fall. And Zufar said: she is divorced three, considering arithmetic, and by arithmetic it would be four, but divorce does not exceed three, so three falls. (Mukhtalas, Fath) 'And if he intended' is connected, meaning whether he intended the calculation or nothing. (T, A)
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage discusses the phrase 'you are divorced two in two' (ثنتين في ثنتين). The Hanafi ruling is that two divorces fall, regardless of whether the husband intended the arithmetic operation. If he intended 'in two' as a container, it is invalid, so the contained (two) falls. If he intended multiplication, the effect is only to multiply parts, which does not increase the count of divorces beyond two. Zufar disagrees, saying that multiplication gives four, but since divorce is capped at three, three fall. The Hanafi position is that only two fall because the multiplication is seen as a figure of speech, not creating additional divorces. The commentary also notes that 'if he intended' (ونوى) is connected to the previous ruling, covering both intention of arithmetic and no intention.

Key Terms
ثنتين في ثنتين (ithnatayni fī ithnatayn): Phrase meaning 'two in two', ambiguous between container and multiplication.
ظرف (zarf): Container expression; used here to refer to the particle 'in' as a locative.

Key Takeaway

Attaching Divorce to Place: From Here to Syria and In Mecca
Rulings on place-attached divorce with dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for 'from here to Syria' and 'in Mecca', explain the dispute with Zufar, and apply the principle that divorce is not localized.

Core Idea
Divorce is not spatial; place words like 'in Mecca' make it immediate, not conditional.

This passage presents two key rulings on attaching divorce to a place. The first, 'from here to Syria', involves a dispute with Zufar over whether the divorce is revocable (Hanafi) or bain (Zufar). The second, 'in Mecca', is ruled immediate, with similar examples. Understanding how the Hanafi school uses rational proof (dalil aqli) to reject localization of divorce is essential for distinguishing immediate (tanjiz) from conditional (ta‘liq) divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
First, locate the phrase 'من هنا إلى الشام' (from here to Syria) and note the Hanafi ruling: one revocable. Then locate 'بمكة' (in Mecca) and note the ruling: immediate. Recognize that 'إذا دخلت مكة' (if you enter Mecca) is conditional.
These are distinct categories: metaphorical distance vs. place specification.
2
Analyze the dispute with Zufar
Zufar argues that 'from here to Syria' describes the divorce as long, making it bain. The Hanafi response: divorce is not a body, so length is metaphorical; it refers to revocability. Also, the phrase 'to Syria' may refer to the woman, not the divorce.
The Hanafi proof is aqli (rational): divorce occurs in all places, so shortening can only be legal (revocable).
3
Apply the principle for 'in Mecca'
The text states that 'you are divorced in Mecca' is immediate because divorce is not specific to a place. Extend this to similar phrases: 'in the house', 'in a garment', etc.
The legal attribute (وصف حكمي) is considered like a real attribute for immediate effect.
4
Distinguish between tanjiz and ta‘liq
Note the critical difference: 'in Mecca' (tanjiz) vs. 'if you enter Mecca' (ta‘liq). The former is immediate, the latter conditional on entry.
This distinction is central to Hanafi divorce law.
5
Synthesize the legal principle
Conclude: place words attached to divorce are interpreted as immediate unless they clearly indicate a condition (e.g., 'if'). The Hanafi school prioritizes the non-spatial nature of divorce.
This principle is derived from the nature of divorce (talāq) as a verbal act not tied to location.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن هنا إلى الشام واحدة رجعيّة، وبمكة كان أي فالواقع تطليقتان أي يقع طلقة واحدة الله المعين) وعند واحدة إن لم ينو: ... من هنا إلى الشام: أي لو قال: أنت طالق من ههنا إلى الشام فهي واحدة رجعية عندنا، وقال زفر هي واحدة بائنة؛ لأنه وصف الطلاق بالطول، ولا يرد عليه أنه إذا قال لها : أنت طالق تطليقة طويلة يقع الرجعي عنده فكيف يقع البائن هنا؛ لأنه يقول: هنا يثبت الطول بالكناية وبها يقع بائنا بخلاف الصريح، ولنا أنه وصف الطلاق بالقصر ؛ لأن الطلاق متى وقع، وقع في الأماكن كلها، ونفس الطلاق لا يحتمل القصر؛ لأنه ليس بجسم فقصر حكمه بكونه رجعياً، وقيل: إن قوله: "إلى الشام" للمرأة دون الطلاق حتى لو قال: تطليقة إلى الشام بزيادة لفظ تطليقة يكون بائناً. رمز الحقائق: ۲۳۸/۱](مستخلص) وبمكة إلخ: أي إذا قال لها : أنت طالق بمكة، أو قال: في الدار يقع الطلاق في الحال في كل البلاد والأماكن؛ لأن الطلاق لا يختص بمكان دون مكان، وكذا لو قال: أنت طالق في ثوب كذا أو في الشمس أو في الظل، أو عريضة أو مصلية فيقع في الحال؛ لأن اختصاص الطلاق بهذه الصور وصف حكمي فيعتبر بالحقيقي، والحقيقي . =  أو في مكة أو في الدار تنجيز ، وفي إذا دخلت مكة تعليق. فصل في إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان ط دع أنت طالق غداً أو في غدٍ تطلق عند الصبح ونية العصر تصح في الثاني، وفي اليوم غداً أو غدا اليوم
And from here to Syria: one revocable [divorce]. And in Mecca, it was... so the actual is two divorces, i.e. one divorce falls. 'Allah the Helper' and according to 'one if he did not intend'... 'From here to Syria': that is, if he said: 'You are divorced from here to Syria', it is one revocable divorce according to us. Zufar said it is one bain because he described the divorce as long. It is not objected that when he says 'you are divorced a long divorce', it is revocable according to him; so how does it become bain here? He says: here the length is established by metaphor and by that it becomes bain, unlike the explicit. Our proof is that he described the divorce as short because whenever divorce occurs, it occurs in all places, and divorce itself does not admit shortness because it is not a body, so the shortening of its ruling is by it being revocable. It is said that his words 'to Syria' refer to the woman, not the divorce, so if he said 'a divorce to Syria' adding the word 'divorce', it becomes bain. (Mukhtalas) 'And in Mecca etc.': that is, if he said to her: 'You are divorced in Mecca', or said: 'in the house', divorce occurs immediately in all lands and places because divorce is not specific to one place. Similarly, if he said 'you are divorced in such-and-such garment' or 'in the sun' or 'in the shade', or 'a garment' or 'a prayer mat', it occurs immediately because the specification of divorce to these forms is a legal attribute and is considered as real. [Page 408] 'Or in Mecca or in the house': immediate. 'And in if you enter Mecca': conditional. Chapter on attaching divorce to time. 'You are divorced tomorrow or on tomorrow': she is divorced at Fajr. And the intention of 'Asr' is valid in the second. And 'today tomorrow' or 'the day of today'.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage covers two place-related rulings. First: 'from here to Syria' (من هنا إلى الشام). According to the Hanafis, this yields one revocable divorce because the husband described the divorce by distance, but divorce is not spatial; thus it is interpreted as a metaphor for revocability (shortness). Zufar says it is one bain (irrevocable) because the metaphor suggests length, which in Hanafi fiqh makes the divorce bain. The Hanafi response is that length/shortness in divorce is only considered in its legal effect (revocable vs bain), not physically. Second: 'in Mecca' (بمكة) is immediate, meaning the divorce takes effect at once everywhere, because divorce cannot be localized. Similarly for 'in a garment' etc. However, 'if you enter Mecca' (إذا دخلت مكة) is conditional, so divorce is suspended on entry. This distinction between immediate and conditional is key.

Key Terms
تنجيز (tanjīz): Immediate effect of divorce, not suspended on a condition.
تعليق (ta‘līq): Suspension of divorce on a condition, e.g., 'if you enter Mecca'.
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce; husband cannot revoke during iddah.
رجعي (raj‘ī): Revocable divorce; husband may take wife back during iddah without new contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Only when the wife enters the mosque
C) Only if the husband is also in the mosque
D) It does not take effect because divorce cannot be attached to a place

Key Takeaway
Place words attached to divorce (e.g., 'in Mecca') produce immediate divorce; distance metaphors (e.g., 'from here to Syria') are interpreted as revocable, not conditional or bain.
