Divorce for Tomorrow: Ghadan vs Fi Ghadin
Ruling on divorce added to 'tomorrow' with and without 'fi'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce added to 'tomorrow' vs 'on tomorrow' and explain the difference in judicial acceptance of intention.

Core Idea
Without 'fi', 'tomorrow' means the whole day; with 'fi', it is a container—intention to delay is court-accepted only in the latter.

When a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow,' does the divorce occur at dawn? Yes, in both 'ghadan' and 'fi ghadin'. But what if he intends the end of the day? The Hanafi school, particularly Imam Abu Hanifa, draws a sharp distinction based on the presence of the preposition 'fi'. This lesson unpacks that linguistic nuance and its consequences in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two utterances
Consider the two phrases: 'You are divorced tomorrow' (ghadan) and 'You are divorced on tomorrow' (fi ghadin). Note that both refer to the next day.
The difference is the preposition 'fi'.
2
Understand the default occurrence at dawn
In both cases, the divorce occurs at dawn of the following day. This is because the woman is described as divorced throughout all parts of tomorrow, which can only be realized by it occurring at the earliest part.
This is the default ruling.
3
Analyze the effect of intention on judicial vs religious acceptance
If the husband intends the end of the day, his intention is accepted religiously (diyanatan) in both phrases. However, judicially (qada'an), it is accepted only when he used 'fi ghadin' because 'fi' indicates a container that does not require totality.
Without 'fi', 'tomorrow' implies totality, so specification is not accepted in court.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with Sahibayn
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn) hold that intention is not accepted judicially in either case, as both 'ghadan' and 'fi ghadin' are containers. Abu Hanifa's view is that the letter 'fi' explicitly indicates containment.
Sahibayn argue that 'tomorrow' in both forms is a container of time.
5
Apply the mufta bihi position
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is Abu Hanifa's view, as it is more cautionary and consistent with the literal meaning of 'fi'.
This is the view favored by later Hanafi authorities like al-Marghinani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان

أنت طالق غداً أو في غدٍ تطلق عند الصبح ونية العصر تصح في الثاني، وفي اليوم غداً أو غدا اليوم لا يختص بها، ويصدق في الكل ديانة لا قضاء ولو قال عنيت إذا دخلت مكة وإذا لبست ونحو ذلك يصير معلقاً كقوله : أنت طالق إلى الشتاء أو إلى رأس الشهر عندنا خلافاً لزفر
Chapter on Adding Divorce to Time

You are divorced tomorrow or on tomorrow: she becomes divorced at dawn, and the intention of the afternoon is valid in the second [case]. And [in the case of] 'today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today' it is not specific to it. And he is believed in all cases religiously but not judicially. And if he said: I intended 'when you enter Mecca' or 'when you wear' and similar, it becomes suspended like his saying: You are divorced until winter or until the beginning of the month according to us, contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq begins a new chapter on adding divorce to a specific time. The first ruling deals with saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' (ghadan) versus 'on tomorrow' (fi ghadin). In both cases, the divorce occurs at dawn of the next day, because the woman is described as divorced throughout all parts of tomorrow, which can only be realized by it occurring at the earliest part. However, there is a difference in the validity of a later intention: if he says 'on tomorrow' (bi-fi) and intends the end of the day, his intention is accepted religiously and judicially because the particle 'fi' indicates a container, which does not necessitate totality; occupying any part suffices. But if he says 'tomorrow' (without fi), and intends the end, he is believed only religiously, not in court, because the word 'tomorrow' without fi implies the entire day, and specifying a part is a qualification of a general statement, which is metaphorical and not accepted when it lightens the ruling. The same applies to 'today tomorrow' meaning he said 'You are divorced today tomorrow': the first mentioned time prevails, so if he says 'today tomorrow' the divorce occurs today; if 'tomorrow today' it occurs tomorrow. The ruling 'belief in all cases religiously not judicially' applies to these cases. Then the matn mentions that if he later says 'I intended when you enter Mecca' etc., it becomes suspended (mu'allaq), like saying 'until winter' – this is the Hanafi position against Zufar who would treat it as an immediate divorce.

Key Terms
غداً (ghadan): The word 'tomorrow' without a preposition, implying the entire day from dawn to dawn.
في غدٍ (fi ghadin): 'On tomorrow' with the preposition 'fi', indicating a container that can be partially occupied.
ديانة (diyānatan): Religiously, before God; the husband's inner intention is accepted between him and God.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, if a husband says 'You are divorced tomorrow' (ghadan) and later claims he intended the afternoon, is his intention accepted in court?
A) Yes, because 'tomorrow' is a container.
B) No, because 'tomorrow' without 'fi' implies totality.
C) Yes, because intention is always accepted.
D) No, because divorce must be immediate.

Key Takeaway
The presence of the preposition 'fi' in 'on tomorrow' makes the time a container, allowing judicial acceptance of intention to delay, unlike 'tomorrow' without it.

Time-Related Divorce Rulings: Today/Tomorrow and Suspension
Rulings on 'today tomorrow' and conditional intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on 'today tomorrow' and 'tomorrow today' statements and how a conditional intention converts a time-based divorce into a suspended (mu'allaq) divorce, with the ikhtilaf with Zufar.

Core Idea
When two times are mentioned, the first governs; and a later intention to condition the divorce on an event converts it to a suspended divorce, not an immediate one.

A husband might say 'You are divorced today tomorrow' or 'tomorrow today'—which time applies? The Hanafi rule is that the first mentioned time prevails. Additionally, if the husband later says 'I intended when you enter Mecca' or similar, the divorce becomes suspended (mu'allaq), like saying 'until winter,' contradicting Zufar's view that it would be immediate. This lesson covers these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase 'today tomorrow' and 'tomorrow today'
Consider the utterances: 'You are divorced today tomorrow' (al-yawm ghadan) and 'You are divorced tomorrow today' (ghadan al-yawm). Note the order of the two times.
The phrase literally juxtaposes two days.
2
Apply the first-mentioned time rule
If he says 'today tomorrow', the divorce occurs today (the first mentioned). If he says 'tomorrow today', it occurs tomorrow. The first time mentioned takes effect.
This is based on the principle that the first word governs.
3
Understand the general principle of religious vs judicial belief
In all cases (including these), the husband is believed religiously if he claims a different intention, but not judicially.
This is the same principle from the previous lesson.
4
Analyze the case of conditional intention
If the husband later says 'I intended when you enter Mecca' or 'when you wear a garment', the divorce becomes suspended (mu'allaq), meaning it is conditional upon that event. This is analogous to saying 'You are divorced until winter.'
The intention retroactively turns the divorce into a suspended one, not immediate.
5
Examine the ikhtilaf with Zufar
Zufar holds that such a statement constitutes an immediate divorce, not a suspension. The Hanafi school (including Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) holds that it becomes suspended.
Zufar's view is rejected by the majority.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq begins a new chapter on adding divorce to a specific time. The first ruling deals with saying 'You are divorced tomorrow' (ghadan) versus 'on tomorrow' (fi ghadin). In both cases, the divorce occurs at dawn of the next day, because the woman is described as divorced throughout all parts of tomorrow, which can only be realized by it occurring at the earliest part. However, there is a difference in the validity of a later intention: if he says 'on tomorrow' (bi-fi) and intends the end of the day, his intention is accepted religiously and judicially because the particle 'fi' indicates a container, which does not necessitate totality; occupying any part suffices. But if he says 'tomorrow' (without fi), and intends the end, he is believed only religiously, not in court, because the word 'tomorrow' without fi implies the entire day, and specifying a part is a qualification of a general statement, which is metaphorical and not accepted when it lightens the ruling. The same applies to 'today tomorrow' meaning he said 'You are divorced today tomorrow': the first mentioned time prevails, so if he says 'today tomorrow' the divorce occurs today; if 'tomorrow today' it occurs tomorrow. The ruling 'belief in all cases religiously not judicially' applies to these cases. Then the matn mentions that if he later says 'I intended when you enter Mecca' etc., it becomes suspended (mu'allaq), like saying 'until winter' – this is the Hanafi position against Zufar who would treat it as an immediate divorce.

Key Terms
معلقاً (mu'allaqan): Suspended; a divorce that is conditional upon a future event.
إلى الشتاء (ila al-shita'i): Until winter; an example of a time-based suspension.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent Hanafi jurist known for dissenting opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The divorce becomes suspended until she graduates.
C) The divorce occurs tomorrow as per the second time.
D) The divorce is invalid due to ambiguity.

Key Takeaway
In time-based divorce, the first mentioned time governs, and a later conditional intention converts the divorce into a suspended one, a distinct Hanafi ruling contrary to Zufar.

Divorce Suspended by Later Intention
Ruling on when husband claims he intended a condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school accepts a husband's later intention to suspend a divorce and distinguish this from Zufar's view.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a later declaration of intent can retroactively suspend a divorce.

Imagine a husband says 'You are divorced' and later insists he meant 'only if you enter Mecca'. Does the divorce take effect immediately, or is it suspended? This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq addresses this exact scenario. The Hanafi school treats the divorce as suspended (mu'allaq), giving weight to the husband's stated intention. We will unpack the reasoning and compare it with the dissenting view of Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Determine the legal classification of the husband's statement when he later claims he intended a condition. According to the Hanafi school, it becomes a suspended divorce (ta'liq).
The divorce does not occur until the condition is fulfilled.
2
Understand the analogy
The passage compares this case to saying 'You are divorced until winter'. Both are treated as suspended divorces. Identify the common element: the presence of a future event as a condition.
This analogy is the basis of the qiyas.
3
Analyze the dispute with Zufar
Zufar holds that the divorce occurs immediately because the original utterance was unconditional. Contrast the proof: the Hanafi school relies on accepting the husband's explanation, while Zufar prioritizes the literal utterance.
The practical consequence: under Hanafi, no divorce until condition; under Zufar, immediate divorce.
4
Apply the ruling
Given a scenario, state the Hanafi ruling and the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference). For example, if the condition never occurs, the marriage remains intact.
This illustrates the importance of intent in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قال عنيت إذا دخلت مكة وإذا لبست ونحو ذلك يصير معلقاً كقوله : أنت طالق إلى الشتاء أو إلى رأس الشهر عندنا خلافاً لزفر
And if he said: I intended 'when you enter Mecca' or 'when you wear' and similar, it becomes suspended like his saying: You are divorced until winter or until the beginning of the month according to us, contrary to Zufar.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage addresses a scenario where the husband pronounces a general divorce (e.g., 'You are divorced') and then later says 'I intended (a condition such as) when you enter Mecca' or 'when you wear such-and-such'. According to the Hanafi school, this statement transforms the divorce into a suspended (mu'allaq) divorce, meaning it will only take effect upon the occurrence of the condition. The analogy is drawn to saying 'You are divorced until winter' or 'until the beginning of the month', which are also treated as suspended divorces in the Hanafi school. This is contrary to Zufar, who would consider the divorce as immediate and not suspend it. The reasoning is that the husband's later explanation indicates that his original utterance was intended as a conditional statement, and since the condition is not yet fulfilled, the divorce is suspended. The condition is considered as implied from the beginning due to his intention.

Key Terms
معلقاً (mu'allaqan): Suspended; a divorce that is contingent upon the occurrence of a future condition.
عنيت (‘anaytu): I intended; the husband's declaration of his inner intent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The divorce is void because the intention contradicts the utterance.
D) The husband must repeat the divorce after the condition occurs.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a husband's later declaration of intent can suspend a divorce, preventing it from taking effect until the stated condition is met.

Divorce with Negative Conditions: 'As Long As' vs 'If Not'
Rulings on divorce expressions 'as long as I do not divorce you' and 'if I do not divorce you'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between temporal and conditional negative particles in divorce expressions and state the Hanafi ruling for each.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, 'ma lam' triggers immediate divorce; 'in lam' does not—because the former is temporal, the latter conditional.

In this passage, we examine how subtle differences in Arabic phrasing can radically change the legal outcome of a divorce statement. The words 'ma lam' (as long as not) and 'in lam' (if not) both contain a negation, but Hanafi jurisprudence treats them differently based on their grammatical function. Understanding this distinction is crucial for any student of Hanafi family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sets of expressions
Separate the phrases using 'ma lam' and its synonyms from those using 'in lam' and its synonyms.
The Arabic text lists three temporal forms and three conditional forms.
2
Classify the grammatical function
'Ma lam', 'mata lam', and 'mata ma lam' are adverbs of time (zarf zaman). 'In lam', 'idha lam', and 'idha ma lam' are conditional particles (huruf al-shart).
This classification is the basis of the Hanafi ruling.
3
Apply the Hanafi principle
Temporal adverbs specify a time: 'as long as I have not divorced you' means the moment of speech is that time (since he has not divorced her), so the divorce takes effect immediately. Conditional particles create a future condition that remains pending; silence does not fulfill the condition, so no divorce.
The ruling is based on understanding of 'ma' as temporal versus 'in' as conditional.
4
Derive the ruling
For the temporal set: divorce occurs at the moment of utterance. For the conditional set: divorce does not occur by mere utterance or silence.
The text states 'taliqat' and 'la'.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Other schools may treat all negative particles as conditional, leading to different outcomes. Hanafi scholars rely on hadith and linguistic analysis to maintain the distinction.
The Hanafi position is mufta bihi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنت طالق ما لم أطلقك أو متى لم أطلقك أو متى ما لم أطلقك طلقت، وفي إن لم أطلقك أو إذا لم أطلقك أو إذا ما لم أطلقك لا.
You are divorced as long as I do not divorce you, or when I do not divorce you, or whenever I do not divorce you – she becomes divorced. And [in] 'if I do not divorce you' or 'if I do not divorce you' or 'if I do not divorce you' – she does not become divorced.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between two types of negative conditional expressions. The first set uses words like 'ma lam' (as long as not), 'mata lam' (when not), and 'mata ma lam' (whenever not). These expressions are considered adverbs of time (zarf zaman) and thus imply a specific time free from divorce. When the husband utters such a phrase and then remains silent, the condition (absence of divorce) is immediately fulfilled because at the moment of utterance he has not divorced her, so the divorce takes effect at that moment. The second set uses 'in lam' (if not), 'idha lam' (if not), and 'idha ma lam' (if not) – these are considered conditional particles (حروف الشرط) that suspend the divorce until a future time when the condition is violated. Since he has not yet divorced her at the moment of speaking, the condition is not yet violated, so the divorce does not occur. Only if he later divorces her would the condition be violated? Actually, the condition is negative: 'if I do not divorce you' – if he never divorces her, the condition remains true indefinitely, so the divorce never occurs. If he divorces her later, then the condition (that he did not divorce her) becomes false at that future time, triggering the divorce? The text says here it does not happen (la). The commentary explains that 'in' and 'idha' are conditional particles, and the condition is a lack of divorce; at the time of utterance, he has not divorced her, so the condition is not false yet; it remains a future possibility. Some interpretations hold that if he divorces her later, the condition is met and the second divorce occurs? But the text says 'la' – it does not happen. The commentary (not fully shown) would clarify that the 'in lam' expressions do not cause immediate divorce, and the husband's silence does not trigger it because the condition is not yet violated.

Key Terms
ما لم (mā lam): 'As long as not'; considered a temporal adverb that triggers immediate effect upon silence.
إن لم (in lam): 'If not'; considered a conditional particle that suspends the divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says 'You are divorced if I do not divorce you' (in lam utallik), and then remains silent, what is the ruling?
A) The divorce occurs immediately.
B) The divorce never occurs from this statement.
C) The divorce occurs after one year of silence.
D) The divorce occurs if he later divorces her.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between temporal adverbs and conditional particles determines whether a negative divorce condition is immediately effective or remains pending.

Attaching Divorce to a Time Before Possession: Ruling of Laghw
Ruling on divorce attributed to time before marriage or to yesterday

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a divorce pronouncement is considered laghw (void) due to attribution to a time before marital ownership, and apply the Hanafi rule for attaching divorce to two time references.

Core Idea
A divorce attributed to a time before marriage is laghw; but if marriage preceded that time, it is an immediate divorce.

Divorce is a serious act that requires marital authority at the time of utterance. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi rulings when a husband attributes his divorce to a time before he had ownership over his wife—such as saying 'You are divorced yesterday' when he married today. The passage also explains the principle for when divorce is attached to two different times using a conjunction, a preliminary rule that sets the stage for the main laghw discussion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first-mention rule
When divorce is attributed to two times (e.g., 'today and tomorrow'), the first mentioned time governs. If the present time is first, one divorce occurs now; if the future time is first, it occurs later. This is because the first mention establishes the ruling, and the second cannot recondition it.
This rule applies only when using a conjunction (waw).
2
Define laghw in divorce
A divorce pronouncement is laghw (void) if it is attached to a time when the husband had no marital authority—e.g., before marriage, before his own existence, while he was a child, insane, or asleep.
Laghw means the utterance produces no legal effect.
3
Distinguish the slave exception
If the husband says to his slave 'You are free before I bought you', the slave becomes free because it is an admission of freedom. This contrasts with divorce, which requires ownership at the time of utterance and cannot be based on admission alone.
Admission is a proof in law; ownership is required for divorce.
4
Apply the insha rule
If the past time mentioned actually falls within the marriage period (e.g., he married before yesterday and says 'You are divorced yesterday'), the pronouncement is treated as an immediate creation (insha) and divorce occurs now, because it cannot be interpreted as a truthful report.
The distinction between insha and khabar is crucial here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان
يعتبر الأول: [ أي اللفظ المذكور أولاً فيقع في اليوم غدا في اليوم، وفي غدا اليوم في الغد. (ع)] بأنه حيث ذكر الأول ثبت حكمه تنجيزا أو تعليقا فلا يتغير بذكر الثاني؛ لأن المعلق لا يقبل التعليق، ولو عطف بالواو وقال: "أنت طالق اليوم وغداً " أو "أنت طالق غداً واليوم" تقع واحدة في الأول وثنتان في الثانية، والأصل أنه أضاف الطلاق متى إلى وقتين كائن ومستقبل بحرف عطف، فإن بدأ بالكائن فالطلاق واحد وإن بدأ بالمستقبل تعدد الطلاق، الشافعي في قول: يقع غداً أخرى في اليوم غدا، وعند زفر : يقع واحدة فيهما جميعاً. [رمز الحقائق: ٢٣٩/١] لغو: أي قال لامرأته أنت طالق قبل أن أتزوجك أو قال: أنت طالق أمس ولم ينكحها أمس بل نكحها اليوم فكلامه لغو؛ لأنه أضاف الطلاق إلى وقت لم يكن مالكاً له فيه فلغا، كما إذا قال: أنت طالق قبل أن أخلق، أو قبل أن تخلقي، أو طلقتك وأنا صبي، أو نائم، أو مجنون، وجنونه كان معهوداً، بخلاف ما إذا قال لعبده: أنت حر قبل أشتريك، أو أنت حر . أمس وقد اشتراه اليوم حيث يعتق عليه لإقراره بحريته قبل ملكه، ألا ترى أن قال لعبد غيره: أعتقك مولاك ثم اشتراه يعتق عليه؛ لإقراره، ولأنه يمكن لصحيحه إخباراً عن عدم النكاح أو عن كونها مطلقة بتطليق غيره من الأزواج. (عيني، مص) وقع الآن: أي لو نكحها قبل أمس وبعده قال : أنت طالق أمس وقع الطلاق الساعة؛ لأنه لم يسنده إلى حالة منافية، ولا يمكن تصحيحه إخباراً لكذبه وعدم قدرته على الإسناد، فكان إنشاء والإنشاء في الماضي إنشاء في الحال فيقع الساعة. (مستخلص، فتح)
Book of Divorce. Attaching Divorce to Time. The first [word] is considered: meaning the first mentioned wording, so it occurs today if he says 'tomorrow today' and tomorrow if he says 'today tomorrow'. Because when the first is mentioned, its ruling is established as immediate or conditional, so it is not changed by mentioning the second; because the conditional does not accept conditioning. And if he joins with waw and says 'You are divorced today and tomorrow' or 'You are divorced tomorrow and today', one divorce occurs in the first [case] and two in the second. The principle: he attached divorce to two times, present and future, with a conjunction. If he begins with the present, the divorce is one; if he begins with the future, the divorce is multiple. Al-Shafi'i in one opinion: another [divorce] occurs tomorrow in the case of 'today tomorrow'; and according to Zufar: one divorce occurs in both times. [Ramz al-Haqaiq 1/239] Laghw (void): i.e., if he says to his wife 'You are divorced before I marry you' or he says 'You are divorced yesterday' while he did not marry her yesterday but married her today, his speech is laghw; because he attached the divorce to a time when he had no ownership over her, so it is void, as if he said 'You are divorced before I was created' or 'before you were created' or 'I divorced you while I was a child' or 'sleeping' or 'insane' and his insanity was habitual. Contrary to if he says to his slave 'You are free before I buy you' or 'You are free yesterday' while he bought him today, where he becomes free due to his admission of his freedom before his ownership. Do you not see that if he says to the slave of another 'Your master freed you' then he buys him, he becomes free due to his admission. And because it is possible for it to be correct as information about the absence of marriage or that she was divorced by the divorce of another husband. (Ayni, Mus) 'It occurs now': i.e., if he married her before yesterday and after that he says 'You are divorced yesterday', the divorce occurs at the moment; because he did not attribute it to a contradictory state, and it cannot be corrected as information due to its falsehood and his inability to attribute, so it is a creation (insha) and creation in the past is creation in the present, so it occurs at the moment. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq with its commentary addresses the ruling when a husband attributes divorce to a time before he had marital authority. The author first establishes the general principle: when divorce is attributed to two different times using a conjunction, the first time mentioned is given effect. For example, if he says 'You are divorced today and tomorrow', one divorce occurs today; if he says 'You are divorced tomorrow and today', one divorce occurs tomorrow (the first mentioned). This is because the first mention establishes the ruling as immediate or conditional, and the second mention cannot alter it—a conditional cannot be conditioned again. Al-Shafi'i in one opinion holds that a second divorce occurs tomorrow in the latter case, and Zufar holds that one divorce applies to both times. Then the passage explains the specific ruling of laghw (voidness): if a man says 'You are divorced before I marry you' or 'You are divorced yesterday' while he married her today, the statement is void because it attaches divorce to a time when he was not the owner of the wife. This is analogous to saying 'You are divorced before I was created' or while he was a child, sleeping, or insane—all are void for lack of capacity. An exception is made for the case of a slave: if he says 'You are free before I buy you' or 'You are free yesterday' and he buys him today, the slave becomes free because it is an admission of freedom before ownership, and admissions are binding. This differs from divorce because divorce requires ownership at the time of utterance, whereas emancipation can be based on admission. Finally, if he marries her before yesterday and then says 'You are divorced yesterday', the divorce occurs immediately at the moment of utterance. This is because it cannot be interpreted as a report (since it would be false), so it is treated as an immediate declaration (insha) of divorce in the present.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): Legally void utterance that produces no effect, here used for divorce attributed to a time when the husband lacked ownership.
إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان (idafat al-talaq ila al-zaman): Attaching the divorce pronouncement to a specific time, such as 'today', 'yesterday', or 'tomorrow'.
إنشاء (insha): Creation of a legal effect through utterance; here, a divorce pronounced in past tense is treated as an immediate creation if it cannot be a report.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife on Monday, 'You are divorced yesterday' and they were married on Sunday. What is the Hanafi ruling?
A) The divorce is laghw because it is attributed to yesterday.
B) The divorce takes effect immediately on Monday as an insha.
C) The divorce is conditional and takes effect on Sunday.
D) The divorce is suspended until the wife confirms.

Key Takeaway
A divorce pronouncement attached to a time before marriage is void (laghw); but if the marriage already existed at that time, it is an immediate divorce via insha.

The Effect of Arabic Particles in Conditional Divorce: Time vs. Condition
Ruling on 'ma lam', 'mata', and 'in lam' phrases in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between time-denoting and condition-denoting particles in conditional divorce and state the Hanafi ruling for each, including the intra-madhhab dispute over 'idha'.

Core Idea
The Arabic particle used in conditional divorce determines whether divorce is immediate or delayed until death; 'idha' is disputed between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

In Hanafi fiqh, the precise wording of a conditional divorce is crucial. The same structure can yield different rulings depending on whether the particle indicates time or condition. This lesson examines the key particles—'ma', 'mata', 'in', and 'idha'—and the scholarly disagreement over 'idha'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particle and its default meaning
For each conditional phrase, classify the leading particle (ma, mata, in, idha) as primarily time or condition based on Arabic usage.
Refer to the key terms for definitions.
2
Apply the Hanafi rule for time vs. condition
If the particle is purely for time, divorce occurs immediately upon silence; if for condition, divorce is delayed until the condition becomes impossible (usually at death).
3
Examine the dispute over 'idha'
Abu Hanifa holds 'idha' is shared between time and condition, causing doubt; the Sahibayn hold it is primarily for time. Understand each side's dalil and the thamarah al-khilaf.
The dispute only arises when no intention is specified.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa: divorce does not occur immediately due to doubt, so it occurs at death unless the husband intended time.
If the husband intends condition or time, his intention is followed by agreement or consensus.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنت طالق ما لم أطلقك أو متى لم أطلقك أو متى ما لم أطلقك أي لو قال لامرأته وسكت طلقت، وفي إن لم أطلقك أو إذا لم أطلقك أو إذا ما لم أطلقك لا، أي الزوج بعد التكلم هذا أي فيقع الطلاق أي لا تطلق وعند حتى يموت أحدهما أنت طالق ما لم أطلقك أنت طالق طلّقت هذه الطلقة، أنت الزوجين كذا يوم أتزوجك فنكحها ليلاً حنث، بخلاف الأمر باليد، أي وقع الطلاق حتى يموت أحدهما [ أي أحد الزوجين قبل أن يطلق عند أبي حنيفة. (ع)] أما في "إن لم أطلقك" فالمسألة اتفاقية؛ لأن كلمة "إن" للشرط حقيقة، وقد علق الزوج الطلاق بعدم الفعل وتحققه باليأس عن الوقوع، وذلك بالموت فيقع في آخر جزء من أجزاء حياته أو حياتها؛ لأنه حينئذ ينقطع إمكان البر فيحنث، وفي "إذا لم أطلقك وإذا ما لم أطلقك" كذلك عند أبي حنيفة وقالا تطلق فيهما حين سكت؛ لأن كلمة "إذا" و"إذا ما" للوقت، قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِذَا الشَّمْسُ كُوِّرَتْ (التكوير (۱) فصار بمنزلة "متى" و"متى ما" ألا ترى أنه لو قال لامرأته: "أنت طالق إذا شئت" لا يخرج الأمر من يدها بالقيام عن المجلس كما في "متى شئت" وبه قالت الثلاثة، ولأبي حنيفة: أن كلمة "إذا" يستعمل في الوقت كما قلتم ويستعمل للشرط أيضاً فكان مشتركاً بين المعنيين. ففي قوله : "إذا لم أطلقك" إن كان بمعنى "منى" يقع الطلاق في الحال، وإن كان بمعنى "إن" يقع في آخر العمر، فوقع الشك في وقوعه في الحال، فلا يقع بالشك، وأما مسألة المشيئة فالطلاق قد تعلق بمشيئتها، فإن كان بمعنى "إن" انقطع تعليقه بانقضاء المجلس، وإن كان بمعنى "متى" لم ينقطع فلا ينقطع بالشك، والخلاف فيما إذا لم ينو أحدهما، فإن نوى الشرط لا يقع في الحال اتفاقاً، وإن نوى الوقت يقع إجماعاً. (مستخلص، عيني)
You are divorced as long as I do not divorce you, or whenever I do not divorce you, or whenever I do not divorce you—i.e., if he says to his wife and then becomes silent, she is divorced. And in 'If I do not divorce you' or 'When I do not divorce you' or 'Whenever I do not divorce you', she is not divorced. i.e., the husband after speaking this - i.e., the divorce occurs - i.e., she is not divorced. And according to [Abu Hanifa] until one of them dies - you are divorced as long as I do not divorce you - you are divorced - she is divorced with this divorce. You are [divorced] on the day I marry you - then he married her at night, he is in violation, contrary to the matter of the hand. i.e., the divorce occurs. Until one of them dies [i.e., one of the spouses before he divorces according to Abu Hanifa. (Ayni)] As for 'If I do not divorce you', the case is agreed upon; because the word 'in' is for condition in reality, and the husband conditioned divorce on non-action, and its realization occurs with despair of occurrence, which is by death, so it occurs in the last part of his or her life; because then the possibility of fulfillment (birr) is cut off, so he violates. And in 'When I do not divorce you' and 'Whenever I do not divorce you' it is similar according to Abu Hanifa, but the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said she is divorced in them immediately upon silence; because the word 'idha' and 'idha ma' are for time. Allah said: 'When the sun is wrapped up' (81:1). So it becomes like 'mata' and 'mata ma'. Do you not see that if he says to his wife 'You are divorced when you wish', the matter does not leave her hand by standing up from the gathering, as in 'whenever you wish'? And the three (Abu Hanifa's disciples) said that. And for Abu Hanifa: the word 'idha' is used for time as you said and is also used for condition, so it is shared between the two meanings. So in his saying 'When I do not divorce you', if it is in the sense of 'when' (mata), the divorce occurs immediately; if it is in the sense of 'if' (in), it occurs at the end of life. Thus doubt arises about its occurrence immediately, so it does not occur due to doubt. As for the issue of will (mashi'a), the divorce is suspended on her will. If it is in the sense of 'if', its conditioning is cut off by the end of the gathering; if in the sense of 'when', it is not cut off, so it is not cut off by doubt. The dispute is when he did not intend either. If he intended condition, it does not occur immediately by agreement; if he intended time, it occurs by consensus. (Mustakhlas, Ayni)
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage deals with the effect of various Arabic particles used in conditional divorce. When a husband says 'You are divorced as long as I do not divorce you' (ma lam utilizing at-talaq) or using 'mata lam' or 'mata ma lam', the divorce takes effect immediately upon the husband's silence after the utterance. This is because these particles denote a time (zaman) that is free from divorce; as soon as he finishes speaking, that time exists, so she is divorced. However, if he uses 'in lam' (if I do not divorce), the divorce does not occur immediately but is delayed until the end of his or her life, because 'in' is a conditional particle, and the condition (not divorcing) becomes impossible to fulfill only upon death. There is agreement on this among the Hanafis. For 'idha lam' and 'idha ma lam', Abu Hanifa holds the same ruling as 'in lam'—i.e., divorce occurs at death—while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) hold that divorce occurs immediately upon silence, because 'idha' is a time particle like 'mata'. They cite Quranic usage (When the sun is wrapped up) to support that 'idha' denotes time. They analogize to the case of 'You are divorced when you wish': if she wishes in the gathering, it takes effect, but if she leaves the gathering without wishing, the condition lapses—this shows 'idha' is for time, not condition. Abu Hanifa responds that 'idha' is shared between time and condition, causing doubt. Since the husband's intention is unknown, divorce cannot be established by doubt. However, if the husband explicitly intends condition or time, his intention is followed by agreement or consensus respectively. The passage also briefly mentions the case of 'until one of them dies' (hatta yamuta ahaduhuma), referring to a similar conditional scenario.

Key Terms
إن (in): Conditional particle meaning 'if'; used in a divorce condition that delays the effect until the condition is fulfilled or becomes impossible.
إذا (idha): A particle that can mean 'when' (time) or 'if' (condition); in divorce, its meaning is disputed.
متى (mata): Particle meaning 'when' or 'whenever', clearly denoting time; causes immediate divorce upon silence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'You are divorced when I do not divorce you' without any intention. According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when does the divorce take effect?
A) Immediately upon his silence.
B) At his death.
C) Never.
D) Only if he intended time.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi conditional divorce, the particle determines timing: 'ma' and 'mata' are immediate, 'in' is at death, and 'idha' is disputed but the relied-upon position (Abu Hanifa) defers to death due to ambiguity.

Combining Divorce Conditions: Immediate Pronouncement vs. Pause, and Divorce on the Day of Marriage
Rulings on consecutive divorce pronouncements and divorce on the day of marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on consecutive divorce pronouncements with a conditional oath, and distinguish between the interpretation of 'day' in divorce oaths vs. delegation of divorce power (amr bi al-yad).

Core Idea
Istihsan exempts the time needed to fulfill the oath from the condition; 'day' in divorce includes night as time, but in delegation it means daytime.

This lesson addresses two distinct but related issues in Hanafi divorce law. First, when a husband combines a conditional divorce oath with an immediate pronouncement, the timing and intention determine how many divorces occur. Second, the word 'day' in divorce oaths is interpreted broadly to include night, unlike in delegating divorce power to the wife. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi use of istihsan and contextual interpretation of texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario: conditional oath followed by immediate pronouncement
The husband says: 'You are divorced as long as I do not divorce you' (a conditional suspended divorce) and then immediately says 'You are divorced'. Determine whether the second pronouncement occurs during a time free from divorce (the time of speaking) and whether a pause changes the outcome.
The key is whether the second utterance is connected to the first or separated by a pause.
2
Understand the Hanafi position vs. Zufar's position
According to Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, if the second pronouncement is immediate (no pause), only one divorce occurs (the second). If there is a pause, two divorces occur if she is mawtu'a. Zufar holds that two occur even without a pause because the time of the second utterance is a time free from divorce, activating the conditional divorce.
The basis of istihsan: the time needed to fulfill the oath is exempted from the condition, similar to a person who swears not to live in a house and then immediately moves out.
3
Analyze the second scenario: divorce on the day of marriage
If a husband says 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced' and marries at night, the divorce takes effect because 'day' here means 'time' (including night). The Quran uses 'day' metaphorically for time.
This is a common metaphorical usage; the literal meaning (daytime) is not intended here.
4
Distinguish from the case of amr bi al-yad
In delegation of divorce power, if the husband says 'Your matter is in your hand on the day so-and-so arrives' and he arrives at night, the wife does not get the power. Here 'day' is taken literally (daytime) because the context requires a specific period.
The difference lies in the intent: in divorce oaths, the intent is to specify a time; in delegation, it is to specify a period during which the wife can act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
طلقت هذه الطلقة: [يعني الطلقة المستفادة من قوله: أنت طالق الذي في آخر الكلام. (ع)] أي إذا قال: "أنت طالق ما لم أطلقك" وقال متصلاً: "أنت طالق" طلقت هذه الثانية، وإن سكت ساعة وقع ثنتان إن كانت موطوءة، وقال زفر: يقع ثنتان سواء سكت أم لا إن كان مدخولا بها وهو القياس؛ لأنه أضاف الطلاق إلى زمان خال عن التطليق وقد وجد، وإن كان قليلاً، وهو زمان قوله: "أنت طالق" قبل أن يفرغ من التكلم به، وجه الاستحسان: أن زمان البر غير داخل في اليمين؛ لأن البر هو المقصود لا يمكنه تحقيق البر إلا أن يجعل هذا القدر مستثنى عن اليمين كما لو حلف لا أسكن هذه الدار فاشتغل بالنقلة من ساعته وأصل الخلاف فيمن حلف لا يلبس هذا الثوب وهو لابسه فإنه لا يحنث إذا نزعه في الحال عقب اليمين عندهم وحنث عند زفر. (عيني، مص، فتح) أنت كذا: أي لو قال لامرأته: أنت كذا أي طالق (ع) فنكحها ليلاً حنث: أي من قال لامرأته: "يوم أتزوجك فأنت طالق" فتزوجها ليلاً طلقت؛ لأن المراد باليوم هنا الوقت ويشمل الليل والنهار، وقوله: "بخلاف الأمر باليد" بأن قال الزوج: "أمرك بيدك يوم يقدم فلان"، فقدم ليلاً فلا خيار لها، والفرق أن اليوم يطلق على أحدهما: النهار، وهو من طلوع الصبح الصادق إلى غروب جرم الشمس شرعاً، ومن طلوع جرم الشمس إلى غروبه عرفاً، وهو معناه الحقيقي، وثانيهما: مطلق الوقت وهو معناه المجازي، وقد شاع إطلاقه فيهما كما قال الله تعالى: ﴿وَمَنْ يُوَلِّهِمْ يَوْمَئِذٍ دُبُرَهُ ﴾ (الأنفال:١٦) أي وقتئذ، وقال: بايام الله ) (إبراهيم:ه) أي بأوقات نعمائه
She is divorced with this divorce: meaning the divorce derived from his saying 'you are divorced' which is at the end of the speech. (Ayni) That is, if he says 'You are divorced as long as I do not divorce you' and then says connectedly 'You are divorced', this second [divorce] occurs; and if he pauses for a while, two occur if she was sexually accessible (mawtu'a). Zufar said: two occur whether he pauses or not if she was entered into, and that is the qiyas (analogy); because he attached the divorce to a time free from divorce, and it has occurred even if it was small, and that is the time of his saying 'you are divorced' before he finishes speaking. The basis of istihsan: that the time of fulfilling (birr) is not included in the oath; because fulfillment is the objective; he cannot achieve fulfillment unless this amount is exempted from the oath, like if he swore 'I will not live in this house' and then busied himself with moving immediately. And the origin of the dispute is regarding one who swore 'I will not wear this garment' while he is wearing it: he does not break the oath if he removes it immediately after the oath according to them (Abu Hanifa and the two), but Zufar holds he breaks the oath. (Ayni, Mus, Fath) 'You are such': i.e., if he says to his wife 'you are such' meaning divorced, then he marries her at night, he breaks the oath. That is, whoever says to his wife 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced' and then marries her at night, she is divorced; because the intended meaning of 'day' here is time, which includes night and day. He said: 'Contrary to the matter of the hand', i.e., if the husband says 'Your matter is in your hand on the day so-and-so arrives' and he arrives at night, then she has no choice. The difference is that 'day' can refer to one of two: the daytime, which from true dawn to sunset shar'an, and from sunrise to sunset customarily, which is its real meaning; and the second: absolute time, which is its metaphorical meaning. And it has become common in both usages, as Allah said: 'And whoever turns his back on that day' (8:16) meaning at that time; and He said: 'By the days of Allah' (14:5) meaning times of His blessings.
Source pages p. 410

Commentary
This passage addresses two related issues. First, the combination of two divorce pronouncements: if a husband says 'You are divorced as long as I do not divorce you' (which creates a conditional divorce that takes effect immediately upon silence) and then immediately says 'You are divorced', the second divorce takes effect at that moment. However, if he pauses for a while between the two statements, two divorces occur if she is mawtu'a (sexually accessible). Zufar argues that even without a pause, two divorces occur because the time of the second utterance is a time free from divorce, so it should count as a separate divorce. Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn use istihsan: the time needed to fulfill the oath (by pronouncing the second divorce) is exempted from the condition, similar to a person who swears not to live in a house and then busies himself with moving out immediately—he is not in violation. The second issue: if a man says 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced' and marries her at night, the divorce takes effect because 'day' here is interpreted broadly as 'time', including night. This is a common metaphorical usage in the Quran. However, in the case of 'amr bi al-yad' (giving the wife the power of divorce), if the husband says 'Your matter is in your hand on the day so-and-so arrives' and he arrives at night, the wife does not get the power, because the word 'day' in that context is taken in its real meaning (daytime), as the hand (power) is only effective during the daytime. The difference is based on the context: in divorce, the intent is to specify a time, so it is interpreted broadly; in giving the wife authority, it is to specify a specific period, so it is taken literally.

Key Terms
البر (al-birr): Fulfillment of the oath; here, the husband fulfills his oath not to divorce by pronouncing a divorce that counts as the promised divorce.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows an exception to a general analogy for a stronger reason; used to exempt the time of pronouncing the second divorce from the condition 'ma lam utalliquka'.
الأمر باليد (al-amr bi al-yad): A delegation of divorce power to the wife; the husband says 'your matter is in your hand' meaning she can divorce herself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife (mawtu'a): 'On the day I marry you, you are divorced.' He marries her at night. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) She is not divorced because night is not part of 'day'.
B) She is divorced because 'day' here means time, including night.
C) She is divorced only if the marriage contract was written during the day.
D) The divorce is suspended until daytime.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan exempts the time needed to fulfill an oath from its condition; 'day' in divorce oaths is interpreted broadly as 'time', but in delegation of power it is taken literally.

The Meaning of 'Yawm' in Conditional Talaq
Explains the legal meaning of 'yawm' in talaq statements

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between prolonged and non-prolonged actions in talaq conditional statements and determine the meaning of 'yawm' according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
With non-prolonged actions like marriage, 'yawm' means absolute time; with prolonged actions like arrival, it means daytime.

When a husband attaches talaq to a time condition using the word 'yawm' (day), the ruling depends on whether the associated action is prolonged (mumtadd) or instantaneous (ghayr mumtadd). The Hanafi school establishes a rational criterion to resolve the ambiguity, drawing on Quranic usage and the nature of the action. This lesson unpacks that criterion and its application through concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic ambiguity
Recognize that 'yawm' in Arabic can mean either the daylight period or absolute time (including night). The author begins by noting both usages.
The Quranic verse (62:9) uses 'yawm' to refer to daytime, but other verses may use it for absolute time.
2
Apply the criterion: prolonged vs. non-prolonged
If the action associated with 'yawm' is prolonged (e.g., fasting, travel), it takes a period and thus 'yawm' is interpreted as daytime. If the action is non-prolonged (e.g., marriage, divorce), no period can be assigned, so 'yawm' means absolute time.
The criterion is based on whether the action 'accepts timing' (qabil li al-tawqit).
3
Examine the examples
Work through the two examples given: 'You are divorced on the day I marry you' (non-prolonged → absolute time) and 'Your hand is on the day of Zayd's arrival' (prolonged → daytime). Determine the outcome in each case.
For the marriage example, marrying at night triggers the condition; for the arrival example, arriving at night does not trigger the condition.
4
Consider the husband's intention
Note that if the husband claims he intended the daytime meaning when saying 'the day I marry you', his claim is accepted in court because it is the literal meaning of his words, even though it eases his obligation.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of giving weight to the declarant's intention in ambiguous statements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أحدهما عن المدة - وبلائه، فأريد به في هاتين الآيتين مطلق الوقت، ويراد بياض النهار أيضاً قال الله تعالى: ﴿إِذَا نُودِيَ لِلصَّلاةِ مِنْ يَوْمِ الْجُمُعَةِ (الجمعة:٩) فإن المراد به النهار، وإذا شاع استعماله فيهما فلابد ضابطة يمتاز به من الآخر، فنقول : إذا قرن باليوم فعل ممتد يراد به بياض النهار والمراد بالممتد ما يقبل التاقيت أي يصح ضرب له كالأمر باليد والصوم، وإذا قرن به فعل لا يمتد أي ما لا يقبل التاقيت ولا يصح ضرب المدة له كالطلاق والتزوج يراد به مطلق الوقت ففي أنت طالق يوم أتزوجك" المراد باليوم الوقت؛ لأنه أضيف إلى فعل غير ممتد وهو التزوج، فيحنث لو نكح ليلاً، وفي الأمر باليد يوم قدوم زيد مثلاً لا يكون أمرها بيدها؛ لأن اليوم أضيف إلى فعل ممتد وهو قدوم زيد؛ لأن القدوم يكون بالسير وهو ممتد، ولو قال الزوج عنيت بيوم أتزوجك بياض النهار خاصة دين في القضاء؛ لأنه نوى حقيقة كلامه وإن كان فيه تخفيف على نفسه. (مستخلص، زيلعي)
One of them [the two meanings] concerns the period... and [the word 'yawm' is] used in these two verses to mean absolute time, and also means the brightness of the day. Allah Most High says: 'When the call is made for prayer on Friday' (al-Jumu'ah 9) and what is meant here is the daytime. Since its usage is common in both, a criterion is needed to distinguish one from the other. We say: if a prolonged action is associated with 'yawm', what is meant is the brightness of the day, and 'prolonged' means that which accepts timing, i.e., a period can be assigned to it, like the command regarding a hand [in the case of making the wife's divorce conditional on a hand action] and fasting. If an action that is not prolonged is associated with it—i.e., that which does not accept timing and for which a period cannot be legitimately assigned, like divorce and marriage—then what is meant by 'yawm' is absolute time. Thus in 'You are divorced on the day I marry you', what is meant by 'day' is [absolute] time; because it is associated with a non-prolonged action, which is marriage. So he [the husband] would be in breach [of his oath] if he marries at night. And in the case of the command regarding a hand on the day of Zayd's arrival, for example, she does not have her hand [i.e., the option of divorce] because 'day' is associated with a prolonged action, namely Zayd's arrival, because arrival involves traveling, which is prolonged. If the husband says: 'I meant by "the day I marry you" specifically the brightness of the day', his word is accepted in judicial ruling because he intended the literal meaning of his words, even though it reduces [the obligation] on himself. (Extracted, Zayla'i)
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof section from a Hanafi fiqh work (likely Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary). It addresses the legal interpretation of the word 'yawm' (day) when used in conditional divorce statements. The author notes that 'yawm' has two common meanings: (1) absolute time, and (2) the daylight period. To determine which is intended, a criterion is established based on the nature of the action associated with 'yawm'. If the action is prolonged (mumtadd) and can be timed (like fasting or the act of giving a hand in the case of tafwid), 'yawm' means daylight. If the action is non-prolonged and cannot be timed (like divorce or marriage), 'yawm' means absolute time. The proof is drawn from the Quranic usage in Surah al-Jumu'ah where 'yawm' refers to the daytime. The commentary provides an example: if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced on the day I marry you', and he marries at night, he has broken his conditional divorce (if he swore to divorce her on that day) because 'day' here means absolute time, so even night counts. Conversely, if he says 'I make your hand [in divorce] contingent on the day of Zayd's arrival', since arrival is a prolonged action, 'day' means daylight, so the option is not triggered if Zayd arrives at night. If the husband claims he intended 'daylight' in the first example, his claim is accepted in court due to the principle that one's intention is given weight in ambiguous statements, especially when it eases the obligation. (Note: This is a Hanafi ruling; other schools may differ.)

Key Terms
فعل ممتد (fi'l mumtadd): An action that is prolonged and can be timed, such as fasting or traveling.
فعل غير ممتد (fi'l ghayr mumtadd): An action that is instantaneous and cannot be timed, such as marriage or divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced on the day I marry you', and he marries at night, does the divorce take effect according to the Hanafi school?
A) Yes, because 'day' means absolute time.
B) No, because 'day' means daytime only.
C) Yes, because marriage is a prolonged action.
D) No, because the condition is not fulfilled.

Key Takeaway
The meaning of 'yawm' in conditional talaq hinges on the nature of the associated action: prolonged actions trigger the daytime meaning; non-prolonged actions trigger the absolute time meaning.

Talaq — Divorce

The Hanafi Ruling on 'I Am Divorced from You'
Inter-madhhab dispute on the phrase 'ana minki taliq'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrase 'ana minki taliq' and explain the Hanafi proof from the narration of Ibn Abbas.

Core Idea
Saying 'I am divorced from you' is legally void—divorce removes the wife's restraint, not the husband's.

When a husband says to his wife 'I am divorced from you' (ana minki taliq), a dispute arises between madhabs about whether divorce occurs. The Hanafi school rules that this statement is nonsense (laghw) and produces no effect, even if the husband intended divorce. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi position, its proof from the narration of Ibn Abbas, and the counter-argument from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
The Hanafi school holds that the phrase 'ana minki taliq' is laghw (void) and does not effect divorce, regardless of intention.
This is based on the principle that divorce removes restraint (qayd) from the wife, not the husband.
2
Identify the opposing view
Al-Shafi'i, Malik, and Ahmad argue that divorce occurs if the husband intended it, because marital ownership is shared.
They see divorce as removing ownership, which can be attributed to either spouse.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi school relies on the narration of Ibn Abbas, where a woman given the power of divorce said 'You are divorced' and Ibn Abbas corrected her, implying the restraint is on the wife.
The key evidence is that divorce is for removing the wife's restraint, so attributing it to the husband is ineffective.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, a Hanafi judge would not count this as a divorce, while a Shafi'i judge would if intention is proven.
This affects subsequent marital status and remarriage possibilities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا منك طالق أي لو قال لامرأته: أنا منك إلخ فهو لغو لا يقع به الطلاق. (ع) لغو وإن نوى متصلة أي ولو نوى الطلاق. (ع)] أي إذا قال لامرأته: 'أنا منك طالق' فهذا لغو ليس بشيء وإن نوى الطلاق، وقال الشافعي: يقع إذا نوى؛ لأن ملك النكاح مشترك بين الزوجين حتى تملك المطالبة بالوطي كما يملك الزوج المطالبة، والطلاق وضع لإزالة ملك النكاح فيصح مضافاً له كما يصح مضافاً إليها، وبه قال مالك وأحمد ولنا أنه شرع لإزالة القيد، والقيد عليها لا عليه وعلى هذا الخلاف إذا ملكها الطلاق، فطلقت الزوج، والحجة عليه ما ورد عن ابن عباس لما في امرأة جعل زوجها أمرها بيدها في الطلاق الثلاث، فقالت: أنت طالق ثلاثاً، فقال ابن عباس : خطأ الله ،نوءها لو قالت: أنا طالق ثلاثاً لكان كما قالت، وإنما قلنا: القيد عليها؛ لأنها . الممنوعة من التزوج. (مص، فتح) في البائن والحرام: [ أي لو قال: أنا منك بائن. (ع)] أي إذا قال لها : أنا منك بائن أو أنا عليك حرام بانت منه بالاتفاق؛ لأن الإبانة والتحريم إزالة الوصلة، وهي مشتركة بينهما، وهذا إذا أضاف الحرمة إليها، ولو قال: 'أنا بائن، ولم يقل: 'منك' أو قال: 'أنا حرام ، ولم يقل: 'عليك' لم تطلق وإن نوى الطلاق؛ لاحتمال أن يكون له امرأة أخرى فيريدها بذلك رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٠/١] (فتح، مستخلص)
'I am divorced from you' means if a man says to his wife: 'I am from you' etc., it is nonsense (laghw), and divorce does not occur through it. (Ayni) 'Laghw even if he intended' connected, meaning even if he intended divorce. (Ayni) That is, if he says to his wife: 'I am divorced from you', this is nonsense, nothing happens even if he intended divorce. Al-Shafi'i said: It occurs if he intended, because the ownership of marriage is shared between the spouses such that she can demand intercourse just as the husband can, and divorce is instituted to remove the ownership of marriage, so it is valid when attributed to him just as it is valid when attributed to her. Malik and Ahmad also held this view. Our evidence is that divorce is legislated to remove the restraint (qayd), and the restraint is upon her, not upon him. On this difference of opinion [also depends] the case where he gives her the power of divorce and she divorces the husband. The proof against him is what is related from Ibn Abbas: when a woman whose husband had placed the matter of triple divorce in her hand said: 'You are divorced three times', Ibn Abbas said: 'Allah made her intention wrong; if she had said "I am divorced three times," it would be as she said.' And we only say the restraint is upon her because she is the one prevented from marrying [others]. (Misbah, Fath) Regarding the irrevocably divorced and the forbidden: [Ayni: if he says 'I am irrevocably divorced from you'] that is, if he says to her: 'I am irrevocably divorced from you' or 'I am forbidden to you', she becomes irrevocably separated from him by consensus, because irrevocable divorce and prohibition remove the connection, which is shared between them. This is when he attributes the prohibition to her. But if he says: 'I am irrevocably divorced' without saying 'from you', or 'I am forbidden' without saying 'to you', she is not divorced even if he intended divorce, because it is possible that he has another wife and means her. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/240) (Fath, Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the phrase 'I am divorced from you' (ana minki taliq). The Hanafi school rules that this statement is null and void (laghw) and no divorce occurs, even if the husband intended divorce. This is because the 'restraint' (qayd) of marriage is on the wife, not the husband; divorce is designed to remove the restraint from her. The Shafi'i school, joined by Malik and Ahmad, argues that divorce can be attributed to the husband because marital ownership is shared. The Hanafi proof is a narration from Ibn Abbas: when a woman given the power of divorce said 'You are divorced three times', Ibn Abbas corrected her, implying that if she had said 'I am divorced', it would be effective, but since she said 'you are divorced', it was ineffective because the restraint is on her. The passage also covers the related case of saying 'I am irrevocably divorced from you' (ana minki ba'in) or 'I am forbidden to you' (ana alayki haram): here there is consensus that separation occurs because the connection is mutual. However, if these phrases lack the preposition 'from you' or 'to you', no divorce occurs because the statement might refer to another wife.

Key Terms
لغو (laghw): A statement that is legally void and produces no effect.
ملك النكاح (milk al-nikah): Ownership of marriage, the right of the spouses over each other.
القيد (al-qayd): The restraint of marriage that prevents the wife from remarrying.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a husband says to his wife 'I am divorced from you' (ana minki taliq) intending divorce, what is the ruling?
A) One valid divorce occurs.
B) Three divorces occur.
C) No divorce occurs; it is nonsense.
D) It is a binding divorce if he intended it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a husband saying 'I am divorced from you' does not effect divorce, as divorce removes the wife's restraint not the husband's.

The Ruling on 'I Am Irrevocably Divorced from You' and 'I Am Forbidden to You'
Ruling on 'ana minki ba'in' and 'ana alayki haram'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the phrases 'ana minki ba'in' and 'ana alayki haram' and identify the condition that must be present for divorce to occur.

Core Idea
Saying 'I am irrevocably divorced from you' or 'I am forbidden to you' triggers divorce by consensus, but only with the preposition.

In contrast to the disputed phrase 'ana minki taliq', stronger expressions like 'ana minki ba'in' (I am irrevocably divorced from you) and 'ana alayki haram' (I am forbidden to you) result in divorce by scholarly consensus. However, this ruling hinges on a critical condition: the husband must explicitly direct the statement to his wife using the preposition 'from you' or 'to you'. This lesson explains the consensus, the reasoning behind it, and the practical implications of omitting the preposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrases and the ruling
The phrases 'ana minki ba'in' and 'ana alayki haram' result in an irrevocable divorce (ba'in) by consensus among the schools.
This is because these phrases remove the mutual connection (waslah), which is shared.
2
Understand the reasoning
Unlike 'taliq' which removes the wife's restraint, 'ba'in' and 'haram' remove the relationship itself, which is mutual, so attributing it to the husband is valid.
The passage says: 'al-ibanah wa al-tahrim izalah al-waslah, wa hiya mushtarakah baynahuma'.
3
Spot the condition
The preposition 'minki' (from you) or 'alayki' (to you) must be included. Without it, the statement is ambiguous and no divorce occurs.
If the husband says 'ana ba'in' or 'ana haram' without the preposition, it may refer to another wife, so it is not effective.
4
Apply the exception
If the preposition is omitted, no divorce occurs even if the husband intended divorce, due to the possibility of referring to another wife.
This is a strict rule to avoid confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنا منك طالق أي لو قال لامرأته: أنا منك إلخ فهو لغو لا يقع به الطلاق. (ع) لغو وإن نوى متصلة أي ولو نوى الطلاق. Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'I am forbidden to you' (ana alayki haram). According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) No divorce occurs; it is nonsense.
B) A valid irrevocable divorce occurs by consensus.
C) One revocable divorce occurs.
D) Three divorces occur.

Key Takeaway
The phrases 'ana minki ba'in' and 'ana alayki haram' cause divorce by consensus, but only when the preposition is attached to the statement.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on 'You are Divorced One or No'
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the effect of 'or no' in talaq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the phrase 'you are divorced one or no' and explain why the mufta bihi ruling holds that no divorce occurs.

Core Idea
Doubt in the number of divorces invalidates the entire pronouncement in the Hanafi school.

This lesson examines a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a husband's ambiguous talaq pronouncement: 'You are divorced one or no.' The core issue is whether introducing doubt with 'or' negates the entire divorce or still effects one revocable divorce. We'll explore the reasoning of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf (two views), and Muhammad al-Shaybani, and see why the later established position holds that no divorce occurs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Disputed Phrase and the Positions
The husband says 'anti taliq wahidatan aw la' (you are divorced one or no). Note that Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf say no divorce; Muhammad and early Abu Yusuf say one revocable divorce.
2
Understand Each Imam's Reasoning
For Abu Hanifa: the number is integral; doubt in number invalidates the whole. For Muhammad: doubt only in the number, but the essence 'taliq' remains, so one revocable occurs.
3
Examine the Qiyas (Analogy) Used
Abu Hanifa analogizes to saying 'three' to a non-consummated wife; the number matters. Muhammad distinguishes by saying the number but not the essence is doubted.
4
Apply the Hanafi Principle on Shakk (Doubt)
The principle that doubt in a prerequisite ruling invalidates the ruling. Here, the number is a prerequisite for the occurrence of talaq.
5
Determine the Mufta Bihi and Why
The text indicates the sheikhs' position (Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf) is mufta bihi, based on authority (zahir riwayah) and logical weight of the qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحدة أو لا إلى قوله: 'لغو' ههنا ثلاث مسائل: الأولى أنه لو قال لها: أنت طالق واحدة أو لا، فليس بشيء عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف آخراً، وقال محمد وهو قول أبي يوسف أولاً: تطلق واحدة رجعية؛ لأنه أدخل الشك في الواحدة لدخول كلمة 'أو ' بينها وبين النفي، فيسقط اعتبار الواحدة، ويبقى قوله: 'أنت طالق'، بخلاف قوله: 'أنت طالق أولا' بدون لفظ واحدة؛ لأنه أدخل الشك في أصل الإيقاع، فلا يقع شيء، وللشيخين: أن الوصف هي متى قرن بالعدد فكان الوقوع بذكر العدد ألا ترى أنه لو قال لغير المدخول بها: 'أنت طالق ثلاثاً' تطلق ثلاثاً، .
'One or no' up to his saying 'nonsense.' Here are three issues: The first is that if he says to her: 'You are divorced one or no', it is nothing according to Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad said—and it was the earlier view of Abu Yusuf—that she is divorced one revocable divorce, because he introduced doubt into the one [count] by the entry of the word 'or' between it and the negation, so the consideration of 'one' drops, and his statement 'You are divorced' remains, unlike his saying 'You are divorced at first' without the word 'one', because he introduced doubt into the original occurrence, so nothing occurs. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf): the description, when coupled with a number, the occurrence is by the mention of the number; otherwise, if it were by the description, mentioning three would be nonsense, so it is established that what actually occurs is the described [number] that is omitted. 'You are divorced one' means 'You are divorced one divorce.' So when the occurrence is of the described-by-number, and doubt enters into the occurrence, no divorce occurs due to doubt.
Source pages p. 411

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the phrase 'You are divorced one or no' (anti taliq wahidatan aw la). Abu Hanifa and later Abu Yusuf hold that no divorce occurs at all. Muhammad al-Shaybani (and early Abu Yusuf) hold that one revocable divorce occurs. The reasoning: The word 'or' introduces doubt into the number 'one', so the number is negated, leaving the bare statement 'You are divorced', which would effect one divorce. However, the majority (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) argue that when 'one' is mentioned, the occurrence of divorce is tied to that number; introducing doubt into the number invalidates the entire pronouncement, because the 'description' (the number) is integral to the occurrence. They support this with an analogy: if a man says to a non-consummated wife 'You are divorced three times', three divorces occur; this shows the number is effective. Therefore, doubting the number negates the whole. The text notes that there are three masail; only the first is detailed here.

Key Terms
الشك (al-shakk): Doubt introduced by 'or' that affects the certainty of the number.
الوصف (al-wasf): The description (i.e., the number) that determines the count of divorces.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One revocable divorce occurs.
B) One irrevocable divorce occurs.
C) No divorce occurs.
D) Three divorces occur.

Key Takeaway
Doubt in the number of divorces, introduced by words like 'or', invalidates the entire talaq pronouncement in the Hanafi school.

The Voidness of Talaq Conditioned on Death
Ruling that talaq conditioned on death is void

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling that a talaq conditioned on the death of either spouse is void by consensus and explain the rationale using the concepts of ahliyyah (capacity) and mahalliyyah (subject).

Core Idea
A talaq conditioned on death is void because death negates either the capacity to divorce or the subject of divorce.

This passage addresses a scenario where a husband makes a divorce statement conditional on the death of himself or his wife. The ruling is clear: such a statement is null and void by consensus. The reason lies in the fundamental prerequisites for a valid divorce: the husband must have legal capacity (ahliyyah) to pronounce talaq at the time of pronouncement, and the wife must be a valid subject (mahalliyyah) for it. Death entirely negates these prerequisites, rendering the condition contradictory and the statement ineffective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conditional statement
The husband says 'You are divorced with my death' or 'with your death'. Recognize that the condition is the death of either spouse.
The phrasing may vary but the core condition is death.
2
State the ruling
By consensus of all schools, such a statement is void (laghw) and no divorce takes effect. The hukm is batil.
There is no ikhtilaf on this point.
3
Explain the legal rationale
The condition (death) negates either the husband's capacity (ahliyyah) or the wife's status as the subject of divorce (mahalliyyah). Without both, the act of talaq cannot be valid.
The Hanafi principle: an act requires both capacity and subject at the time of execution.
4
Apply the reasoning
If conditioned on his death: his death removes his capacity to pronounce talaq. If conditioned on her death: her death removes her being a valid subject. Thus the condition renders the pronouncement impossible, not merely suspended.
This differs from conditions like 'if you enter the house' which do not negate capacity or subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع موتي أو مع موتك لغو، والثانية: لو قال لها: أنت طالق مع موتي والثالثة قال لها: أنت طالق مع موتك، فهاتان ليستا بشيء بالاتفاق؛ لأنه أضاف الطلاق إلى حالة منافية للطلاق؛ لأن موته ينافي أهلية الطلاق، وموتها تنافي المحلية، ولابد من كل واحد من الأهلية والمحلية ليثبت جواز التصرف. (مستخلص)
'With my death or with your death' is nonsense. The second: if he says to her: 'You are divorced with my death' and the third: if he says to her: 'You are divorced with your death', these two are nothing by consensus, because he added the divorce to a state that is incompatible with divorce; because his death negates the capacity for divorce, and her death negates the subject of divorce (mahalliyyah), and each one of capacity and subject is necessary for the permissibility of the transaction. (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This is a straightforward ruling stating that if a husband says to his wife 'You are divorced with my death' or 'with your death', no divorce occurs, by consensus of the scholars. The reasoning is that divorce requires both the husband's legal capacity to pronounce divorce ('ahliyyah) and the wife's being the subject of divorce (mahalliyyah). Death negates both: the husband's death removes his capacity; the wife's death removes her being a valid subject. Therefore the condition contradicts the very nature of divorce, rendering the statement null.

Key Terms
الأهلية (al-ahliyyah): Legal capacity to perform acts like divorce.
المحلية (al-mahalliyyah): The quality of being the subject upon which the act operates.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Yes, immediately.
C) No, it is void.
D) It becomes a revocable divorce.

Key Takeaway
A talaq conditioned on death is void by consensus because death negates either the husband's capacity to divorce or the wife's status as the subject of divorce.

Ruling on Annulment of Marriage by Ownership of Spouse
Ruling on annulment of marriage by ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when a marriage contract becomes void due to ownership of a spouse and explain the rational proof (dalil aqli) and the intra-Hanafi difference.

Core Idea
Ownership of a spouse annuls the marriage contract because right-hand ownership is stronger than marital ownership.

This lesson examines the Hanafi position that when a husband or wife acquires ownership of the other, the marriage contract becomes void. We will explore the rational proof based on the strength of milk al-yamin and the exceptions, such as the case of a mukatab. We will also explore the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on one side and Muhammad on the other concerning the effect of divorce after ownership.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State that the marriage contract becomes void (batala) when one spouse owns the other, whether fully or partially.
This applies both to husband owning wife and wife owning husband.
2
Analyze the rational proof (dalil aqli)
Understand that milk al-yamin is a stronger form of ownership that fulfills the purpose of marriage (preserving offspring), thus the weaker marital ownership dissolves. Also note that combining owner and owned prevents marriage.
The proof is qiyas: when necessity is removed by a stronger ownership, the lesser contract is annulled.
3
Examine the exception for a mukatab
A mukatab (slave with emancipation contract) does not have full ownership, only a right to acquire ownership. Therefore, if he buys his wife, the marriage continues.
This shows that the condition for annulment is valid ownership, not mere right to own.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
When the wife owns her husband and he then divorces her: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no divorce occurs because marital ownership no longer exists. Muhammad argues that since 'iddah is required, some marital link remains, so divorce can take effect.
The mufta bihi is the view of the two shaykhs.
5
Determine the practical consequence
In all cases, 'iddah is obligatory after separation due to ownership, even though the marriage is void. Divorce pronouncements after ownership are ineffective.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling that the marriage contract becomes void (batala al-'aqd) when one spouse acquires ownership of the other, whether partially or wholly. The main discussion: if a husband buys his wife (who is a slave) or inherits her, the marriage contract is annulled because the stronger right hand ownership (milk al-yamin) supersedes the weaker marital ownership. Similarly, if the wife owns her husband (e.g., she buys him as a slave), the combination of owner and owned in one party prevents the validity of marriage. The text discusses the reasoning: the purpose of marriage is to preserve offspring, and when ownership is established, that need is fulfilled. It also addresses the issue of partial ownership: even owning a share annuls the contract. A sub-issue: if after ownership the husband divorces her, the divorce does not take effect because marital ownership no longer exists; however, the 'iddah is still obligatory because separation occurred due to ownership. There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding the case of the wife owning the husband: Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf say no divorce occurs; Muhammad says it does because the contract still exists in some sense. But the text favors the two shaykhs. The passage also digresses to repeat the 'death' ruling and the 'one or no' ruling, but the core is about ownership annulling marriage.

Key Terms
ملك اليمين (milk al-yamin): Ownership of a slave (right hand possession).
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has contracted with his master for emancipation in return for payment; he has a right to ownership but not full ownership.
